And that is leading to&nbsp;&nbsp; both a hesitation, but also a very passive behavior.&nbsp; And all those things added up, means that right now&nbsp;&nbsp; the professional wine market is asking a higher&nbsp; risk premium because they do not know what will&nbsp;&nbsp; happen to the market on a short-term basis and&nbsp; therefore they avoid taking up large stock and&nbsp;&nbsp; therefore they will ask to buy at a lower price. So&nbsp; markets are under pressure due to all these things&nbsp;&nbsp; that we are seeing. Another thing worth addressing&nbsp; is the En Primeur, the En Primeur system which for&nbsp;&nbsp; ages has been the primary fine wine trading mechanism in the fine wine world. But the En Primeur lately have seen a lot of challenges.&nbsp;That is why we have referred to it as the Bordeaux Paradox&nbsp;&nbsp; and also why we have advised against investing in&nbsp; the Bordeaux En Primeur. What the case is here&nbsp;&nbsp; is that we have seen chateaus selling wine at&nbsp; the En Primeur to a higher price year after year after&nbsp;&nbsp; year. But at some point the prices have become too&nbsp; high, especially considering that the Bordeaux wines&nbsp;&nbsp; are not that much in demand as they were 50 years&nbsp; ago. So we are having an oversupply in the markets&nbsp;&nbsp; which is eroding the confidence&nbsp; in the market, which has led to overpricing. And&nbsp;&nbsp; to accommodate that, the En Primeur last year last&nbsp; term, the 2023 En Primeur, the producers tried&nbsp;&nbsp; to lower the prices with approximately 25 to&nbsp; 30% for some producers, which is a lot, and what&nbsp;&nbsp; we expect of the 2024 En Primeur which we going into&nbsp; in just a short period of time, well that is prices&nbsp;&nbsp; will be lowered once again. And if that happens&nbsp; which is the most likely scenario right now&nbsp;&nbsp; merchants and collectors who have invested in the&nbsp; recent Bordeaux campaigns may now be holding up&nbsp;&nbsp; stock that is suddenly overpriced relative to the&nbsp; newest release, that will result in a widespread&nbsp;&nbsp; devaluation of the En Primeur holdings. So&nbsp; it's not that secure a place to invest&nbsp;&nbsp; your money and that is why we at RareWine have&nbsp; invested in the or advised against investing in&nbsp;&nbsp; the Bordeaux En Primeur. Another thing, another&nbsp; trend that we have to address&nbsp;&nbsp; when speaking of the wine market, is the wide&nbsp; media speculation about; is consumption declining&nbsp;&nbsp; among the younger generations, and yes in fact it&nbsp; is. The younger generation, so-called generation&nbsp;&nbsp; Z, they do not drink as much wine&nbsp; as the generations before them. So it is correct&nbsp;&nbsp; that we see a consumption that is declining. But&nbsp; what Gen Z is preferring is actually premium&nbsp;&nbsp; wines. They're searching for wines of higher&nbsp; quality. They want quality over quantity &nbsp;&nbsp; So we are more, in the fine wine industry, talking&nbsp; about a 'premiumization' which is really good for&nbsp;&nbsp; the fine wine market, and it's also good for you&nbsp; as a wine investor because the younger generation&nbsp;&nbsp; the, gen Z, they are making conscious choices,&nbsp; they prefer wine that is being produced organic or&nbsp;&nbsp; biodnamic as we are seeing in Burgundy, seeing&nbsp; in Champagne and some parts of Italy as well. And&nbsp;&nbsp; that in general will lead to a search for for&nbsp; better wines and wines of a higher price which in&nbsp;&nbsp; this case is also the wines that are very suitable&nbsp; for investment purposes. Another mega trend that&nbsp;&nbsp; we are facing is shifting diets. We have&nbsp; seen that especially from the Gen Z again,&nbsp;&nbsp; they have health considerations&nbsp; they are having environmental concerns, they have&nbsp;&nbsp; ethics and all kinds of other things&nbsp; leading them to eat more plant-based and more&nbsp;&nbsp; organic diets, and by eating lighter diets, you&nbsp; will also need to consume wine that is lighter&nbsp;&nbsp; that is better for your diet. So the&nbsp; light wine types which are for instance the&nbsp;&nbsp; sparkling wines; it could be champagne, it could&nbsp; also be white wine in general; white burgundy&nbsp;&nbsp; It could also be the more light types of red wines,&nbsp; again for instance, elegant Burgundy wine. They&nbsp;&nbsp; will be preferred in the future more than the&nbsp; more heavier red wines that we saw in the past&nbsp;&nbsp; due to this mega trend of shifting diets. Moving&nbsp; from there to the climate change, which is also a&nbsp;&nbsp; topic in the wine world that we need to address. - What Are the Latest Trends in the Wine Industry for 2023? - Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en What are the latest trends in the wine industry for 2023? In 2023, the wine industry is buzzing with exciting trends that reflected what consumers want today. Let's take a closer look at what's shaping this vibrant market. First off, sustainability is at the forefront. More and more wine lovers are seeking out organic and biodnamic wines. They prefer wineries that use fewer pesticides and recycle water. Vegan wines are also on the rise as they avoid animal products in the production process. Packaging is changing too with lighter glass bottles, cans, and even paperbased bottles becoming popular. This shift helps reduce waste and carbon footprints. Next, convenience is key. Smaller bottles, cans, and boxed wines are gaining traction. These options allow consumers to try different wines without committing to a full bottle. They are perfect for those on the go or for casual gatherings. Younger drinkers in particular appreciate these flexible formats. New world wines are also making waves. Wines from regions like the United States, Australia, New Zealand, and South Africa are attracting attention, especially among those new to wine. These wines often have clear labels in English, making it easier for consumers to find their favorites. They tend to be more affordable and approachable than traditional oldw world wines. The rise of e-commerce is another significant trend. The pandemic pushed many consumers to buy wine online, and this trend continues. Digital platforms are now a major sales channel, allowing wineries to reach more people. Home delivery and virtual tastings are now common, making wine shopping easier than ever. Sparkling wines are enjoying a surge in popularity as well. Drinks like procco and champagne are no longer just for special occasions. Many people are enjoying them at home, especially younger consumers who see them as a fun everyday option. Demographic shifts are also influencing the market. While older generations still buy premium wines, younger consumers like millennials and generation Z are looking for value and sustainability. They are eager to try unique grape varieties and support brands that practice ethical production. On the production side, vineyard areas are slightly decreasing, particularly in the European Union. Countries like Spain and France are reducing their vineyard hectars. Wine production volumes have also dropped in 2023 due to climate factors and market dynamics. Lastly, wineries are engaging more with their communities. Many are partnering with local environmental groups to show their commitment to responsible practices. This not only helps the environment but also builds a positive brand image for wine producers in 2023. Focusing on sustainable practices, innovating packaging, expanding online sales, and appealing to younger consumers are all smart strategies. These trends are helping wineries adapt and thrive in a changing market. [Music]

- The Sommelier's Secret  Decoding the Investment Value of Rare Wine: Kind: captions Language: en Take a moment and pour yourself a glass of wine. What you hold in your hand is a beverage meant for enjoyment, for a quiet evening, a celebration, or a dinner party. Yet for a select few, that same liquid in a different bottle is not a drink, but a high-performing tangible asset. A centuries old commodity now traded in a digital marketplace. We are talking about the secret world of fine wine investment where a single corkked bottle can be worth more than your car, your annual salary, or even your house. This is not about the latest Cabernet you picked up at the store. This is about a highly specialized asset class where scarcity, reputation, and immaculate history converge to create astonishing value. Today, we're decoding the sumeier's secret. What transforms simple fermented grape juice into a multi-million dollar investment? and how this liquid asset class is defying conventional finance. The engine of value, rarity, ageability, and provenence. What separates a $1,000 bottle from a $20 bottle? The answer is simple in theory, complex in execution. Longevity, scarcity, and providence. First, a wine must have the structure to age. Investment grade wines, the creme de la creme of the global market, are built to improve for decades. This means high levels of acidity, balanced tannins, and concentrated fruit designed to soften and harmonize over time. As the wine ages, it enhances its complexity, flavor, and quality, becoming more desirable. Crucially, as the quality improves, the supply diminishes.3. Every time a bottle is consumed, the remaining stock becomes instantly rarer. This creates the classic supply and demand dynamic that drives prices higher. The more coveted and scarcer the bottle, the higher the value it commands on the secondary market. Four, the vast majority of the world's wine is not made for this. Less than 1% of global production qualifies as true investment grade wine. Five. Finally, and perhaps most importantly, there is provenence. Provenence is the documented history of the bottle. Who owned it, where it was stored, and how it was transferred. Six. In a market plagued by sophisticated fraud like the notorious case of Rudy Kernan, Providence is the assurance of authenticity. A bottle with an impeccable paper trail verified by a reputable auction house or storage facility can be worth dramatically more than an identical bottle with an undocumented or questionable past. Seven, this focus on verifiable history ensures that you are buying the legend, not the lie. The Somier's secrets decoding the investment mechanics. Investing in wine works differently than stocks or real estate. It operates largely through two core mechanisms. The entreur system and the secondary market tracked by sophisticated indices. The entreur or wine futures or market is dominated by Bordeaux. Investors purchase wine while it is still in the barrel, often 18 to 24 months before it is bottled and physically released. They receive a certificate of ownership, paying a discounted price in anticipation of the wine's quality and its future scarcity. This system allows merchants to finance the vintage and allows investors to secure bottles from highly sought-after producers before they hit the general market, maximizing their potential profit margin. Once released, bottles enter the secondary market where they are traded between collectors, brokers, and investment platforms. 10. This market is benchmarked by the LiveX London International Ventner Exchange indices such as the Livx 100 or the LiveX 1000.11. These indices track the price performance of the most traded wines, providing transparency and data that was once reserved only for the elite few. 12. This data confirms that fine wine is not merely a hobby. It is a measurable asset class that over the long term has historically offered returns competitive with traditional equities all while acting as a hedge against inflation and broader economic downturns. The trading volume and price movements on the secondary market are what truly determine the investment value of any rare wine. The blue chips regions and producers that command value. The vast majority of investment grade wine originates from a handful of prestigious old world regions. Though the market is steadily diversifying. 13. These are the blue chip names that anchor any serious wine portfolio. Bordeaux remains the cornerstone. 14. The five first growths. Premier Crew Class A Chateau Lafit Rothchild, Chateau Lur, Chateau Muton Rothchild, Chateau Margo, and Chateau Hobo Brion are the equivalent of the Dow Jones Industrial Average. Their track record for quality and longevity in exceptional vintages like 1982, 2000, 2005, and 2009 is unmatched, making them essential reliable investments. Burgundy represents the ultimate expression of scarcity. 15. Due to the fractured ownership of tiny vineyard plots, climate producers like Domain Dea Roman Conti, DRC, Domain Laroy, and Armon Rouso produce minuscule quantities of GR crew wines. When these bottles hit the market, particularly from celebrated vintages, their rarity ensures prices soar, making Burgundy the sector most prone to astronomical returns. 16. The high demand and microscopic supply mean Burgundy prices are often the most volatile, but also the most potentially rewarding. Beyond France, key players include the super tuscans from Italy, such as Sasakaya and Medo, which blend traditional Italian wine- making with French varietals to create internationally acclaimed ageworthy wines. 17 prestige couet champagne led by brands like Dome Perinon and Louis Ruter's Cristal has also solidified its position offering stable returns and a high degree of liquidity. Finally, a small but powerful contingent of Cult Nappa Cabernet, Screaming Eagle, Harland Estate and Scarecrow have established a robust secondary market driven by intense US collector demand and exceptionally low production. These global names are the foundation upon which a fine wine portfolio is built. The crucial role of condition protecting your liquid gold. For a wine to maintain its value, it must be stored in near-perfect conditions. 18. This is where the concept of storing in bond becomes paramount. Storing wine in bond means placing it in a professionally managed temperature and humidity controlled warehouse typically in the UK that has been approved by customs and excise 19. This professional storage protects the wine from the four great enemies heat, light, vibration and temperature fluctuation.20 20 optimal conditions are a steady temperature of around 55° F, 13° C, and 70 to 80% humidity. 21 improper storage, even for a short time, can damage the wine, leading to poor fill levels or cork failure, drastically reducing its value 22. Beyond preservation, in bond, storage provides critical financial benefits. 23 wine stored this way remains exempt from local value added tax and duty.24. When the investor sells the wine, it can be transferred still in bond to another investor or merchant without ever incurring these taxes, making the asset much more attractive and liquid on the international market. 25. For an investor, the paper trail generated by professional bonded storage is the ultimate proof of providence. 26. No serious buyer will touch a wine that has been stored in a private home seller or worse, an unverified location, regardless of the label. Investing in wine means investing in its vault. The risks and rewards of the fine print. As with any asset class, wine investment carries unique risks alongside its substantial rewards. 27. On the risk side, wine is fundamentally a collectible, not a company, and it does not pay dividends. 28. It also carries high associated costs. Professional storage fees, insurance, and brokerage commissions for buying and selling can erode returns if not accounted for. 29. Furthermore, while fine wine is less volatile than many equities, it is an illquid asset. It may take weeks or months to find the right buyer, especially during market downturns. 30. The biggest non-financial risk remains fraud and poor provenence, underscoring the necessity of purchasing only through reputable brokers or auction houses. 31. The rewards, however, are compelling. Wine offers a crucial diversification benefit, often performing independently of the broader stock market, thereby hedging risk. 32. It is a tangible asset that can be used to pass wealth through generations. And unlike stocks, a case of first growth bordeaux from an exceptional vintage is a finite diminishing resource, a true store of wealth. 33. For many, the reward is also emotional. Owning a piece of history, an exquisite work of art that can be opened and enjoyed at its peak maturity. Conclusion: The new portfolio of passion. The investment world has changed. The sumeier secret that the world's finest wines are a stable, high-erforming asset is now out. The modern rare wine market is a fascinating blend of old world tradition and new world technology, leveraging digital indices and bonded warehouses to trade a perishable luxury good. It is a sector where passion meets profit and where the decisions of a few hundred producers in France, Italy, and California determine the value of a global commodity. To invest in rare wine is to invest in patience, history, and the beautiful certainty of finite supply. If this deep dive into the paradoxical world of wine investment captured your imagination, be sure to give this video a like, share this video with a fellow collector or investor, and most importantly, subscribe to our channel for more fascinating looks into the intersection of finance and luxury.

- 6 ESSENTIAL Tips for Building Your PERFECT Wine Collection: Kind: captions Language: en hi everybody this is Agnes from no sediment and today let's explore six important things to consider when building your wine collection [Music] for a wine enthusiast like me collecting wine can be a lot of fun it allows you to study wine in a completely different way furthermore you can be entirely sure of how your wine has been stored throughout its development and eventually you might get the reward of opening and drinking bottles that in 10 years might be difficult to acquire or afford I buy ones that I love wines I know I will want to continue enjoying over the next years but might not be able to afford anymore in my wine cellar you will find a lot of Italian Reds plenty of Austrian whites a bit of American wines and of course Bordeaux grown Loire Wally and a bit of Spain somehow I have never been able to build a decent collection of champagne those bottles always find reasons to be opened and enjoyed as a result I have very little of champagne in my collection but today we are not talking about specific wines or regions to build your collection on but rather some principles we should follow when creating our own wine collection whether you are just starting your wine Journey or are an experienced wine collector or somewhere in between these tips will guide you in building the collection in the right way help you to get most out of this experience and avoid potential disappointments there can be several reasons why you might want to start collecting wine wine can be a great investment and provide an opportunity to diversify your assets in this case you will buy different wines and will need to store them differently if compared to someone like me who collects wine For My Future Self to enjoy later I buy wines for pleasure ones that I am sure I will want to open and drink someday therefore I never buy wine that I don't like personally even though they might be a great financial investment on the other hand if you're considering buying wine for investment purposes you should have the discipline to focus not on wines you personally like but on wines that are most likely to appreciate in value over the years do your research especially if you're collecting wine for investment purposes not all wines from Bordeaux burgundy Tuscany and Napa are investment worthy and certainly not all wines will increase in price therefore understanding the Region's sub-regions single Vineyards clima and producers is highly important for creating a successful collection additionally in-depth knowledge of vintages and keeping a close eye on how the current vintages are shaping in the classic wine regions is essential as well for some wine lovers this might sound like a lot of fun it certainly is for me however it is not just that understanding the market is equally important you need to consider supply and demand as well as weather the wine of your choice will have any liquidity in the secondary Market once you decide to sell it if all this seems overwhelming you might want to consider hiring help or finding a consultant depending on your goals you will need to decide where to store your wines for investment purposes an external inbound storage facility with insurance is a must to maximize your return on investment for a wine lover like me storage could also mean a cool and dark place such as area in the garage or under the stairs for me it is a wine fridge and basically any empty darker Corner in the house however there is an important note to consider for wine investors or really any other Wine Enthusiast who wishes to store their wines with a third party storage expenses should also be factored in not all age-worthy wines that we love so much will be able to cover the yearly costs with that theoretical price increase of the wine therefore it might be necessary to do a little calculation of the expected increase in the value of your collection against the annual costs of wine storage and Insurance it might turn out that it is cheaper to buy your favorite crew Bourgeois with some bottle age sellered in someone else's storage rather than storing it yourself loving and enjoying wine will certainly help you especially if you are creating collection for yourself however if you are buying wine and you know you will want to sell it at some point you should keep your mind rational those who buy wine for investment usually have a full understanding of how much they can invest which wines they want to buy and when exactly to sell them and for investors buying wine in full cases is the smartest choice not only because discounts can be applicable here but also because selling a full case of wine with perfect provenance can be much easier than selling a single bottle for a passion buyers like me it also makes sense to buy wine in full cases as this will allow you to follow Wine's development over the years but buying full cases might not always be possible as it is initially very expensive and can take up a lot of space therefore there might be situations where buying less is actually more another tip for us passion collectors it is to keep the track of number of bottles in your collection because you may already be at the point where even if you drink a bottle of wine per day you still will not be able to clear your seller until the day you die it is important that in one way or another you can track the bottles in your collection it is easy to lose the track of your bottles and as a result end up with wines that are past their Prime whether you buy wine for investment or for future enjoyment tracking your wines including their vintages and drinking windows will help prevent disappointment when you realize that the wine has lived its best life in the storage unit there are several ways to do this some people prefer to do inventory once or twice a year and update their notebooks or Excel spreadsheets While others will follow their wines through online inventory programs in any case it is important to stay focused and disciplined as losing track of your wines is quite easy this of course might come naturally to someone who is investing in wine diversifying your portfolio in terms of producers labels countries Villages vintages and even bottle sizes acts as an insurance against Market fluctuations however it is also important for wine collectors like myself our taste and preferences tend to change and involve over the time therefore don't be too enthusiastic about buying a specific wine style especially if you are only starting your wine collection and wine Journey you might end up purchasing wines that you currently enjoy a lot but not necessarily will want to open and drink with the same enthusiasm later so here you go six important factors to consider when building your wine collection I must admit this is only an introductory video and each of these factors are worthy a video on their own so let me know in the comments what wine collecting or investment topics you would like me to cover in the future videos meanwhile you should definitely watch my other video on seven wine storage and aging myths some might really surprise you or even make you angry cheers foreign [Applause]

When I started building a cellar (in the mid 1980's) I made the conscious decision that for every two bottles I drank I would buy three, it's surprising just how quickly the collection grew. In the 1990's I got the 'En Primeur' bug and was given the best advice ever - buy the best you can, buy three cases, sell two 10 (or more) years later and you'll drink for free. I am fortunate in having a decent cellar, it's in an outside privvy (toilet), the house was built in 1760 and the 'privvy' has 600mm thick solid stone walls, no windows and we have put in a tiled concrete floor, electrics, further insulation and an insulated door, a lot of racking, removed the drains and voila, we have an excellent wine store. It's also in shade under trees so never gets any direct sunlight and I have enough wine stored in it to be able to drink 4 bottles a week for the next 15 years - Sorted!.

- Can the fine wine market maintain its performance? | FT Wealth: Kind: captions Language: en-GB Few would argue that we aren't living in turbulent times. But today I've come to a particularly serene part of southeast England to find out how an evolving fine wine market is responding to an assortment of traditional and not so traditional challenges, ranging from long-term weather patterns to pandemics. It's very strong. The market has moved from being a UK-centric type investment to right now, worldwide. And there are more platforms cycling up all the time. In fact, the Live-ex 100, the leading industry exchange of the secondary market, has risen by around a third over the past two years. They are very, very good at putting out information as to the state of the wine market in general. And they are saying that it's extremely robust. Over the past 10 years, when it comes to luxury investments' performance, wine has trailed only cars and rare whisky, according to the Knight Frank Investment Index. But this global market is facing a global issue that could well affect investment values and prices in the longer term - climate change. The major wine regions for investment are all on the same latitude. So you start off with Napa Valley in California, on the West Coast of the United States, through to Bordeaux, through to Burgundy. And of course, these regions are getting hotter and/or colder. As our climate changes, it could make it harder to produce future vintages of the quality we're used to. Climate change is going to play a part in the wine life. That said, with wine, if there is no more Bordeaux, then the wines that are held with customers are only going to increase in value. Of course, climate change could eventually influence supply the other way by actually opening up new areas for quality wine production. The Champagne region of France, for example, is an area seen as being particularly susceptible to climate change, which is why famed champagne houses, like Pomeroy and Taittinger, are now looking to places they would never previously have considered for production. Big international players have come into the English wine scene to invest in their own vineyards, put their money where their mouth is, and recognise how special the southeast of England is. The effect of climate change will, of course, be gradual. But I have come to the UK's oldest wine merchant, Berry Brothers, founded in 1698, to learn more about how certain aspects of the markets are changing now. Some good ordinary clarets, are they? Yes, my favourite. The company stores around £1bn worth of wine for private clients. There may be a few thousand bottles here, maybe. I actually don't know the exact figure. In Basingstoke we currently have a facility for 9mn bottles. Spending on fine wines actually increased throughout the recent pandemic. And sales for its en Primeur season, which allows collectors to buy the most recent vintage before it is bottled, were up over 40 per cent in 2021 compared with 2019. As sales grow, and with greater volumes being traded across the world, ensuring the origin and care of their wine is as it should be is becoming ever more important for collectors. The bulk of what I do is looking to check that everything that we sell is absolutely as pristine as it should be, and that it's come from the right place so that the provenance is correct, that it's been stored correctly on the way into us. In times of higher inflation, as we're seeing now, physical assets do have the potential to offer solid returns. But wine can entail higher costs than other investments due to factors like shipping and storage. It's seldom a short-term investment, and concerns about counterfeiting are also becoming more prominent. It wasn't really an issue up until about 10 years ago. Now it's something which everyone asks us about. Cases like that of Rudy Kurniawan, who in 2013 was convicted of wine fraud for selling tens of millions of fake wines, have thrown a spotlight on counterfeiting. So how big of a problem is wine fraud nowadays? The more common types of wine fraud is mass produced bottling of perfectly potable table wine with a more expensive label on it. And that's happening probably as we speak. Somewhere in the world, somebody is bottling something which is not what it says on the label. Combating the frauds takes various techniques and technologies, both old and more recent. Can you see the fluorescing lights and the fluorescing label there? Yeah. But there are two tiny little holograms in the shape of a cloverleaf. Their position moves with every vintage. Other verification systems can range from nanochips to tracking numbers and even a system that matches a unique pattern of air bubbles under the cap to an image kept on file. One relatively recent development uses blockchain technology to help gain clarity over a bottle's condition and whereabouts across the supply chain. When you've got a broken supply chain, actually, that's the best time, in my opinion, to commit fraud. So what we're doing is saying, actually, we may make 5,000 or 500, depending on what's going out of a QR code that will be unique to that bottle and that batch. Can you show me how this sort of technology would actually work in practise then? We're going to scan that code as so, and now we're going to go into temperature, humidity, and GPS location of that bottle. Systems like Chainvine won't be for everyone. But in a global survey of wineries last year, blockchain technology ranked second of all technologies they saw as having the highest priority of importance to develop. As for the wine investment market, if it wants to build on strong recent performance, it will have to adapt to the constant changes coming from both inside and outside the industry.

- How the US Wine Industry Became a Billion Dollar Business | Wine Economy USA: Kind: captions Language: en In 1976, a blind tasting in Paris shattered three centuries of European wine supremacy. French judges tasting unmarked glasses declared American wines superior to the most prestigious French vintages. The results were so shocking that one judge tried to take back her scorecard. That single afternoon didn't just embarrass the wine establishment. It revealed the potential for American wine to become not just respected but financially valuable. It hinted at an industry quietly transforming into a billiondoll business that most people had completely overlooked. What happened next wasn't just about better grapes or clever marketing. It was about immigrants, prohibition's long shadow, a few renegade scientists, and the economics of turning fermented fruit into a commodity worth billions. The story of American wine is really a story about how a country built an industry from almost nothing, generated massive financial value, and captured global attention, and along the way, it created investment opportunities few people imagined, where land, barrels, and bottles themselves became assets. Wine in America starts long before anyone thought it could be serious. Spanish missionaries planted the first vines in the 1700s. mostly to make sacramental wine. Thomas Jefferson tried and failed to grow European grapes at Montichello. The soil was wrong. The humidity invited disease and native pests destroyed root systems that had thrived in France for generations. For over a century, American wine meant something sweet, fortified, and forgettable. Then came 1920. Prohibition didn't just stop alcohol production. It dismantled an industry that had been slowly figuring things out. Vineyards were ripped up. Knowledge was lost. Wine makers who understood fermentation chemistry, barrel aging, and varietal selection either left or found other work when prohibition ended in 1933. American wine had to start over with almost no institutional memory and a public that had spent 13 years drinking bathtub gin and Canadian whiskey. Few realized it at the time, but these setbacks were also a financial reset. Land that might have been used for wine was cheap. Investors, dreamers, and entrepreneurs would later see value where others saw ruin. Early vineyards, once considered worthless, would eventually become some of the most expensive real estate in California. For decades after repeal, American wine meant cheap, sweet, and sold in jugs. Most of it came from California's Central Valley, where industrialcale farming prioritized quantity over quality. A gallon of wine cost less than a gallon of milk. Outside America, it wasn't taken seriously. But something was quietly shifting in Northern California. In Napa Valley and Soma County, a few determined individuals started buying land that nobody else wanted. These weren't generational wine makers. They were doctors, engineers, and business people who had fallen in love with European wine and wondered if the right techniques could work in California. They had money, curiosity, and no attachment to tradition. Robert Mandavi opened his own winery in 1966 and he treated wine like a craft and also a potential investment. He invested in French oak barrels, temperature controlled fermentation, varietal labeling and hired university trained enologists. By elevating quality, he increased the perceived market value of every bottle. Other pioneers followed, refining techniques and sharing knowledge, slowly building an industry that outsiders didn't yet notice. The 1976 tasting in Paris was supposed to be a casual promotion. Nine French judges tasted California Chardonnese and Cabernet blind. The expectation was polite acknowledgement, but the results shattered assumptions. A 1973 Chateau Montilena Chardonnay scored highest among whites. A 1973 Stags Leap Cabernet topped the reds. French judges ranked American wines above Muro, Batard Montrache, and Chateau Briion. A single journalist from Time magazine made the results impossible to ignore. Suddenly, wine in California wasn't just good, it had financial potential. Vineyards became assets, bottles became commodities, and a quiet gold rush began in the world of American wine. The Paris tasting didn't just validate California wine, it created a market signal. Premium vineyards became assets and bottles became collectible commodities. Land prices in Napa Valley began climbing. Large corporations bought wineries. Investors entered the market and European producers started forming partnerships. Wine was no longer just a craft. It was big business. Scale created new challenges. How do you maintain quality across thousands of acres? How do you create a consistent product when the weather is unpredictable? American wineries solved these with mechanization, irrigation, lab analysis, and vertical integration. treating wine production like advanced agriculture with financial returns. By the 1990s, the US wine industry generated billions of dollars in annual revenue. California alone produced over 80% of American wine. Brands like Kendall Jackson, Gallow, and Behinger became household names. Wine consumption rose steadily, driven by a growing middle class, lifestyle signaling, and even health research. Collectible bottles entered auctions and investors realized some wines could appreciate like rare art or alternative assets. As the industry grew, it split into two markets. Large producers, millions of cases annually, optimized for consistency, distribution, and revenue. Small producers, limited releases, focused on teroir and critical acclaim, often creating bottles worth thousands. These small wineries depended on the infrastructure and consumer base that the large producers built, but they also represented high value investment assets. The industry had become both accessible and aspirational, generating wealth at multiple levels. Some bottles became investment grade almost overnight. Rare vintages stored under perfect conditions would sell for multiples of their retail price at auctions. Collectors, hedge funds, and wealthy individuals quietly added wine to their portfolios. Vineyards themselves became assets appreciating in value. As global demand for premium wine rose, insurance policies, provenence tracking, and storage facilities turned wine into a physical asset class. a billiondoll ecosystem spanning land, barrels, and bottles themselves. Even modest wineries learned the language of finance. Limited releases, futures markets, and wine clubs created recurring revenue and increased brand value. Investors who understood scarcity and quality could make substantial returns, while consumers gained access to aspirational experiences and cultural capital. Today, the US wine industry generates over $70 billion annually, including production, distribution, retail, tourism, and collectibles. Napa Valley attracts millions of visitors yearly. Wine auctions, collectible bottles, and investment portfolios create a financial ecosystem that extends far beyond the vineyards themselves. Yet, challenges remain. Climate change, water scarcity, shifting consumer habits, and global competition all threaten sustained growth. Wine in America is simultaneously a mass market commodity, artisal luxury, and an emerging alternative asset class. Its future depends not just on taste, but on perception, scarcity, and value. The American wine industry succeeded because a few people believed in a vision and built systems that generated cultural and financial value. Today, wine is simultaneously a drink, a luxury, and an investment. Whether this balance will endure or whether the industry will ultimately have to choose between craft and capital is the billiondoll question. The story began with a blind tasting in Paris. But the most interesting part is what happens next. Who invests? Who drinks? And who defines the value of wine in the years ahead?

Purpose trusts can be set up just to manage and preserve a collection long-term according to your wishes. Grits might help transfer future appreciation out of the estate, though they're complex for collectibles. >> So, having a clear plan for the collection is vital. >> Absolutely vital. A detailed succession plan. What happens after you're gone? Who gets what? Should it be sold? That annual gift tax exclusion, 17K per person, currently lets you transfer smaller pieces or interest over time tax-free, reducing your estate. Sometimes valuation discounts might apply for lack of marketability or partial interest, but you need expert tax advice on that. Sounds like you need a whole team of experts for this. >> You absolutely do. The source emphasized getting advisers who know estate planning, finance, and the specific collectible market specialist, lawyers, appraisers, financial adviserss, trust experts, maybe appointing a knowledgeable trustee or protector to oversee it all. >> What about just donating the collection? >> That's definitely an option. Often a preferred one for major collectors of art or historical items. Donating to museums or institutions can give significant tax deductions. Plus, it preserves the collection for the public and secures a legacy which many collectors care deeply about. >> Okay, that covers the whole arc. - Who Are Some Famous Wine Collectors Around the World? - Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en Who are some famous wine collectors around the world? Have you ever wondered who the most passionate wine collectors are around the globe? These individuals not only have a love for wine, but also invest significantly in rare and valuable bottles. Let's take a closer look at some of these remarkable collectors and what makes their collection stand out. One notable figure is Hardy Rodentock, a German wine connoisseur known for his impressive collection of old and rare wines. He gained fame for acquiring the famous Jefferson bottles, which are believed to date back to the 18th century. One of these bottles, a 1787 Chat Rothschild, was thought to have belonged to Thomas Jefferson and sold for $156,000. However, the authenticity of these bottles is still a topic of debate, adding an air of mystery to his legacy. Another prominent collector is Bill Cotch, an American billionaire with a keen interest in fine wines. His collection includes some of the rarest wines in the world and has been involved in high-profile lawsuits over counterfeit wines, particularly against Hardy Rodentock. Caulox's dedication to preserving fine wines has made him a significant figure in the wine community. Andrew Lloyd Weber, the famous English composer, is also a distinguished wine collector. He developed a serious interest in wine as a teenager. Influenced by his family, his collection features exceptional French wines, including First Growth Bordeaux and Domain Da Roman Ki. Weber has sold large portions of his collection at auctions, generating millions of dollars, showcasing both the quality and value of his holdings. Other notable collectors include Gene Mulvyhill, Michelle Jack Shassi, Leslie Rudd, Yel Briner, Doris Duke, Charlie Troder, and Rudy Kernowen. Each of these individuals has made unique contributions to the world of wine collecting, whether through amassing rare vintages or promoting wine culture. These collectors often focus on wines from prestigious vineyards, rare vintages, and historically significant bottles. Their collections may include ultra rare bottles like Chatau Dem from 1900, Chatau Shioval Blank from 1947 and Domain Da Roman Ki which are highly sought after by enthusiasts worldwide. From a wine perspective, these collectors play an important role in preserving wine heritage and supporting the market. They inspire others to appreciate the artistry and history behind fine wines. Their collections often requiring specialized storage conditions such as climate controlled sellers to maintain the wines's quality over time. Famous wine collectors like Hardy Rodentock, Bill Cotch and Andrew Lloyd Weber have shaped the culture of wine collecting through their passion and expertise. Their stories highlight the blend of history, luxury and the enduring appeal of fine wine.

- Michel Chasseuil: The Man Behind a 50M€ Wine Cellar | FULL DOCUMENTARY: Kind: captions Language: en [Music] In my 20ies, I started taking a few bottles of wine from my parents seller because back then in those days we mostly drank cider. No wonder it took 3,000 concrete blocks and five or six pallets of cement. Plus, to reinforce the cellar, we added 50 cm of surface covering on top of the reinforced concrete slabs with ventilation shafts. That means there's no air conditioning. The natural temperature ranges from 8° in the winter to 15° in the summer. When I was younger, his passion was all consuming. My father also had a second home that we were always fixing up during holidays and weekends. He didn't always have a lot of time to spend with me. We hardly ever went on holiday. Oh, I was joking. By the way, it's not a dungeon. It's where I keep the secret for curve. This is where I make a sort of pen sha. Let's go and check it out together. The nectar. He crafted 33 different varieties using ingredients like holly, slow, rowan, elderberry, truffle, and even garlic. Do you know what's in this case? It's quantro from Guring. This case came all the way from Germany. A case of 12 bottles of quantro. And someday I'll give it a taste. They say that after 70 years it develops a different flavor like the chhatros from Teragona. Hallelujah. Even share us. Here's the complete Petrus collection, ranging from 1945 to today. There's one bottle on display and another in the case. The oldest ones are from 1914, 1928, and 1934. And here you've got them in 12 bottle cases. from 1971 onward. In the 1980s, before the famous Titanic film, I went to an auction in Versailles where they had bottles of 1805 Napoleon cognac and Mari Bizar that was served on the Titanic. The bidding started at 300 Franks, then 400 Franks, and no one seemed interested. I put up my hand, and just like that, I won 10 gold flake marriage bottles. Everyone in the room was laughing. But after the Titanic movie hit the Shaniliz, the Mari Brisar company called me and said, "We would like to buy your Goldflake Mari Brisar bottle for €10,000." And at that auction, there were also bottles of 1805 Napoleon cognac. None of the restaurant owners who were there for the Gong wines showed any interest in the cognac. So I put up my hand and asked if I could get one or two bottles. My friend said, "Grab both. You can sell one to pay for the other." I was low on cash, so I went with two bottles. And that's how I ended up with Napoleon's personal bottles from 1805 from the chat deontl. One day, I got a call from Mr. Shamuti, a brandy producer from Ukraine. He'd heard of me and seen me online and he wanted to invite me to his estate for a 50-year-old brandy tasting. To us, brandy is what we call cognac. During that visit, I met someone who said, "You have bottles from Napoleon's cellar, but I have one from Hitler's cellar." He said, "After the war, while the French were taking the silverware, we raided Hitler's Eagle's Nest and found this bottle. My great-grandfather had it. I asked him how much he earned each month. And he said, " $150." I told him, "Listen, if you find the bottle, I'll pay you a month's salary." So the next day, he came back to the hotel with the bottle, which had a photo of Hitler from 1942. It was a gift Hitler had given to his officers. Here you have a complete collection of EKM from 1900 to 2014 with one bottle for each year. I also keep a second bottle of each in reserve in case we need to recondition any of the bottles. And here's the incredibly rare 1811, which sold for $100,000 in Hong Kong a decade ago. The 1811, 1847, and 1921 are the three most sought after bottles from the last two centuries. Finding all three together is quite a feat. Well, here are the renowned Roman Conti bottles. I have about 40 different bottles with one case per year from 1971 onward. The highlight is the 1945 which went for $500,000 at Christiey's auction in Geneva. Not too long ago, when I bought this bottle in the 80s, it cost me 3 months salary, 115,000 Franks. I never dreamed it would someday be worth $450,000. Early one morning, I got a call. Hello, Mr. Chass. Yes, speaking. This is the principality of Monaco. His highness would like to visit your collection. I thought it was a joke. I said, "Cut it out." I figured it was just a friend playing a prank, but they assured me. No, no, no. I'm Colonel Franon the butler. This is very serious. I had heard from various sources that there was a collector in France gaining some recognition. I thought I'd check if it was possible to meet him. He agreed to meet with me and the small delegation that I brought along. I had a rough idea of his appearance, but he was much shorter than I had imagined. The prince came to see my collection. We were cataloging everything, and I could see he was genuinely bowled over. The experience was astonishing on many levels. The more we explored the seller, the rarer and more incredible the bottles became. I wanted to get his autograph. Of course, an hour passed, then two, then three, and I thought, I guess the autograph isn't going to happen. I asked him about how he managed to get certain cases and bottles. After we left the cellar and moved over to the kitchen, I asked, "Your highness, could I please have your autograph?" And he sat down at the table and said, "Yes, of course, Mr. Shassi." - Getting Into Wine: A Beginner's Crash Course: Kind: captions Language: en Have you ever stared at a wall of wine and had no idea where to start? In the next 20 minutes or so, we're gonna fix that. Hi, my name is Madeline Puckette and I've spent 15 years helping people learn about wine. And if you need some proof, check out Wine Folly's online resource I co-created or my books, which have been printed in 27 languages. The main reason people find it difficult to learn wine is because it's hard to get started. It seems really cool, but it's also overwhelming. How do you choose what you like? Unless you already know a lot, you're essentially rolling the dice with each wine purchase. My goal for you in this video is to give you enough of a foundation so you know how to ask the right questions to drink better wine. After this, choosing a wine shouldn't fill you with low-key dread. Instead, it should feel more like a fun adventure. In short, I want to show you all the things I wish I knew when I was getting started. Being confident in wine helps unlock doors in social situations and it can also help you get a better-paying job. So if you want to make your Wine 101 official, check out Wine Folly's certification courses. Wine is just fermented grape juice, right? This basic concept holds answers that most people overlook and knowing them can help you drink better wine. To answer your question, yes, wine is fermented grapes. Sure, there are wines made with other fruit too, like cranberry wine or pineapple wine, but if it's labeled just wine, that means it's made with just grapes. Of course, the grapes used for making wine look different from table grapes. They're smaller, they're sweeter, they have thicker skins and seeds. These differences give wine its unique taste. Wine grapes also behave differently than other fruit. They don't continue to ripen after they've been picked. And this means wine making happens once a year, right after harvest. And that's what the vintage date means on a wine label. It's the year the grapes were harvested. The vintage is actually where I made my first wine mistake. This was around 2007. I know, it was a long time ago. I bought a bottle of 2005 Côte du Rhône, back when you could get a pretty great red wine for just $13. Côte du Rhône, in case you don't know, this isn't a single-varietal wine like Cabernet Sauvignon. This was a red wine made with three different grapes, Grenache, Syrah and Mourvèdre. And the name Côte du Rhône is the name of the region where the red wine is from. At the time, this was my first Côte du Rhône, and I loved it so much that I ordered a bunch more. The wine arrived from the same region and the same producer, but it wasn't the same vintage. It was the 2006 and not the 2005. And it didn't taste the same at all. I was devastated. Turns out, the weather in each vintage affects how the grapes ripen, which in turn changes the quality of the wine. 2005 was quite warm and dry and produced rich, fruit-forward red wines, which I loved. Yum, yum, yum. 2006, on the other hand, was a little cooler and the critics would call it "fresher," meaning lighter bodied and more tart. The 2006 has ended up aging quite well, but that didn't give me that yum, yum, yum taste I was looking for. We call this vintage variation. It's one of wine's great strengths and great weaknesses. The lesson for me was if I was looking for French red wines like Côte du Rhône, I tended to prefer, at least back then, wines from warmer, drier years. So when you find a wine you like, pay attention to the weather from that vintage. It's a clue. But the thing that most affects how a wine tastes is where it comes from. Over the years, I've mapped thousands of different wine regions around the world. But if you're just getting started, don't try to memorize them. Instead, let's categorize the regions by climate. To do that, I want to go back to where it all started at least 8,000 years ago in the mountainous region of Caucasus. This is where we think winemaking first started. I like to think that the first wine was a complete accident. We had some extra grapes, we put them in a container, forgot about them for a little while, and came back and yuck! Then someone was bold enough to drink it, and this is what started our multi-millenia obsession with wine. Winemaking expanded with Western civilization into the Mediterranean basin. Vines adapted over thousands of years to different climates, cooler climates in the north and hotter climates in the south. And this is why there are thousands of different grape varieties in several different shades. What's surprising is that everything from lush Chardonnay to dry-as-a-bone Sangiovese are all cultivars of the same species. Vitis vinifera, aka the wine grape. And the climate where Vitis vinifera grows is where we get the concept of the wine belt, which includes the latitudes of around 30 degrees north to 50 north and about 30 to 45 south. There are exceptions to the rule, but for the most part, this is where wine grows. And here's the kicker and the big aha. Vines that grow in warmer climates like Shiraz in Barossa, Australia, or Cabernet Sauvignon in Paso Robles, California, tend to produce riper, sweeter grapes, which in turn produces bolder, more fruit-forward wines. In fact, warm climates produce some of the world's best bold red wines. And contrasting that, when you go north, like to Champagne in France or to the Willamette Valley in Oregon, the grapes there produce less sugar content, which results in lighter-bodied, more tart-tasting wines. And guess what? Cool climate regions produce some of the world's best elegant white and red wines. So knowing about warm climate wines and cool climate wines is an absolute game changer. It explains why two wines made from the same grape can taste completely different, and it's a key to consistently finding wine styles that you love. Now, you might be thinking, "This is great, Madeline, but what about all the different grapes? We've got Merlot, Pinot Noir, Chardonnay. It's a lot to remember." And honestly, you're right. With over a thousand different commercially viable wine varieties out there, I wouldn't worry too much about memorizing all of them, save for the ones you love. Instead, we can simplify things again, taking into consideration all the wines of the world, and group them into more or less nine different wine styles. Sparkling wines, light-bodied white wines, aromatic white wines, full-bodied white wines, rosé and orange wine, light-bodied reds, medium-bodied red wines, full-bodied red wines, and dessert and fortified wines. Now, this categorization model covers most of the wine that's out there in the market, so knowing about each of these styles will help you communicate what you want. I'm going to give you an example of what to look for in each category, a great value as well as a benchmark examples. But remember, this is just the tip of the iceberg. Let's start with full-bodied red wines. These are your whole milk, heavy cream of the wine world. However, unlike milk, they don't get their big body from fat content. They get it from alcohol and tannin. What is tannin? It's a plant polyphenol. Sidebar, tannins contains nearly all of wine's health benefits, and texturally, it gives wines layers of complex velvety flavor. Too much tannin in wines taste bitter and astringent, but when it's just right, it's magical. Cabernet Sauvignon is your benchmark bold red wine. In fact, this is the most widely planted wine grape in the world. But if you're looking for value in the U.S., try Petite Sirah. And if you're elsewhere in the world, try Alicante Bouschet from Portugal. These alts aren't as well known about and thus offer great value. Medium-bodied red wines. Have you ever heard someone say, "Oh, that's a food wine." This is what medium-bodied red wines are all about. Instead of being dominated by brooding blueberry and blackberry flavors like a bold red, they tend to have more red fruit notes, like raspberry, cherry, and plum. They also have fresher acidity, which makes them better to pair with food. There are many varieties that fit this category, but the one you should absolutely know about is Côtes du Rhône. This is a regional wine from southern France that blends with Grenache, with Syrah, and Mourvèdre. These three varieties actually grow in regions all around the world in warmer climates. So if you see this blend, snatch it up and give it a try. For value, try this wine from Italy called Montepulciano d'Abruzzo. I consistently cannot believe the quality I find in this wine for the price. Light-bodied red wines. For those of you who hate the bitterness of tannin but love red wine, this is your go-to wine style, and Pinot Noir is your benchmark grape. This wine is so light and delicate you can see your hand through it. They serve it in a big globe-shaped wine glass to capture the airy red fruits and rose aromas that Pinot Noir is all about. Pinot Noir is great, but it's also one of the most expensive red wines out there. So for value, maybe try a bottle of Frappato from Sicily. It has everything we love about light-bodied red wines, but because it's so lesser known about, it tends to be less expensive. So that's red wines. Basically, it's a spectrum from light to bold, and it correlates with the grape variety. And since most red wines are dry, as in not sweet, the fruit factor you may taste on some is more closely associated with the climate where the grape is grown. Cooler climates tend to produce more tart, sour fruit flavors, and warmer climates make wines with riper, more baked fruit notes. Now for the rest of the wine styles. Sparkling wine. It comes in both dry and sweet styles from Brut to Demi-Sec. I like to think of sparkling wine as kind of the most technical winemaker's wine because to make it, it takes two fermentations. One for the alcohol and two for the bubbles. The benchmark is definitely champagne. Champagne is what you would call a regional wine, meaning it can only be made in the region of Champagne in France. And the reason why I say champagne is the benchmark is because they're the ones that perfected a sparkling winemaking method that's been emulated all around the world. But if you're looking for value, try Cava. This is also a regional wine made in Spain, and it uses the same method as champagne, but with Spanish grapes. And also because it's less known about and less popular, it tends to offer better value. Light-bodied white wines. These zippy, dry white wines have citrus and apple notes. And the thing that defines this category isn't the grape variety necessarily, it's that these wines rarely age in oak barrels. Turns out, stainless steel winemakings helps preserve the crisp quality in white wines. Quality for dollar, light-bodied white wines are some of the best wine values out there. Pinot Grigio might just be your benchmark light-bodied white wine. In France, they call it Pinot Gris, and it grows around the world. Looking for a great value? Try Picpoul de Pinet, a regional wine from France. It absolutely sings for the price. Full-bodied white wines. These rich, toasty, dry white wines have lots of body and a creamy texture. Chardonnay is our benchmark. Surprisingly, it's not the Chardonnay grape that gives these wines a creamy, buttery texture. It's the winemaking interaction that happens when Chardonnay ages in oak barrels. In fact, every white wine you find aged in barrels tends to have richer, more full-bodied flavors. If you're looking for value, try Grenache Blanc aged in oak, for an amazing alternative with a very unique taste. Aromatic white wines. There are some white wine varieties out there that are so aromatic that they deserve their own category. They range in taste from bone-dried to sweet, but can easily be mistaken for a sweet wine because of their candied nose of orange blossom and peach. Riesling is your benchmark. And Germany is its home base, making some of the most haunting Riesling in the world. Of course, on the hunt for value, check out Torrontés from Argentina. This wine is typically made dry, but it smells sweet, like potpourri. Rosé and orange wine. Rosé isn't a grape. It's more of a winemaking process using red grapes. Rosé happens when you stop the grape skins from dyeing the wine red by taking them out of the fermentation, midway. And this means Rosé can be made with nearly any red wine grape. So try your favorite red wine as a Rosé, and you'll be surprised by the totally different expression you get from your favorite grape. So what about orange wine? Just like Rosé is a winemaking method using red grapes, orange wine is a method using white wine grapes. Orange wine happens when you ferment a white wine the way you would a red. By leaving the juice fermenting with the skins, the technique is rare, but it makes some of the richest, most red wine-like white wines I have ever tasted. Dessert and fortified wine. Dessert wines range in style from actually pretty dry to very sweet, and sometimes they're stabilized by adding brandy in a process called fortification. Your benchmark is Port wine from Portugal. The longer that some Port ages, the more tawny in color it becomes and nuttier the taste. I once tasted a tawny that was 250 years old and I couldn't believe how good it tasted, especially since most wines don't age past 10 years. For great value, try Sherry from southern Spain. Many Sherry wines are totally dry, salty, and a little bit nutty. It's a totally unique flavor in a wine that you must try at least once in your life. Now that you know the nine common wine styles, you can better communicate and find what sounds good to you. But how do you go about looking the part when you're enjoying a glass of wine? Well, there's some things that sommeliers know that will greatly improve your wine tasting experience. This is a bottle of wine, the world's best invention. And this is maybe the world's second best invention, a waiter's friend. Knowing how to use one of these will make you feel like a real sommelier. Now we have a wine glass. There are many different styles of wine glasses, but a great wine glass has enough space above the wine to collect aromas. You only need 5 ounces or 150 milliliters for a full serving. Now, my glass might look mostly empty, but that's actually the right amount of wine because when I swirl, it releases the aromas into the bowl of glass. When it comes to tasting, I have a whole 10-minute video on the secrets to tasting like a pro, but conceptually wine tasting is much simpler than that. Smell the wine before you taste it. If you block your nose, you won't pick up any of those aromas of black currant, cedar, and peppercorn on a fine Bordeaux. And that's really the secret. It's really that simple. Smell before you taste. If you do this with every new wine you try, you're going to learn so, so much. So let's bring it all together. We started this journey at a massive wall of wine, feeling a little lost. But now you have a powerful new framework. You know that vintage tells you about the weather of a single year and why the same wine can taste different from one year to the next. You've learned that climate is your secret weapon. By knowing whether a wine comes from a warm or cool region, you can predict if the wine will be bold and fruity or light and elegant. And instead of getting bogged down by thousands of grape varieties, you can now confidently navigate the world of wine using the nine core styles. Finally, you've learned that tasting wine isn't actually about snobbery, it's simply about paying attention. The act of smelling your wine before you sip it is the single biggest thing that will elevate your experience and unblock all those amazing flavors that we've been talking about. My promise to you at the beginning of this video was to help you see wine differently. So the next time you walk into a wine shop, that wall of wine won't be so intimidating. Instead of seeing endless confusing options, you see possibilities. You can now ask the right questions. "Hey, I'm in the mood for something from a cool climate." Or "Hey, I really like full bodied red wines, what's a great value alternative to Cabernet Sauvignon?" That feeling? That's confidence. And that's how you start to drink a better wine. Now, if you're excited about what you've learned and want to make your Wine Smarts official, I'd love for you to check out our Wine Folly's certification courses at winefolly.com We have everything from fun, fast beginner courses to deep dives that will get you ready for a sommelier's career. And if you want to continue this journey and learn more about wine with me, make sure you subscribe to this channel because we've got more ground to cover. Thanks for watching and I'll see you next time. Cheers.

  3. @ms97-studio9: Ever wonder why some people have a distaste for those exclusive, ultra-high-end wine spots in New York? There’s a place out there so elite that you basically need to be a celebrity or incredibly wealthy just to get a personalized tasting with the owner. And that kind of exclusivity is exactly why everyday folks feel alienated by these places.
On the flip side, we have people like André Mack, a down-to-earth sommelier who became known through the Bon Appétit YouTube channel. He brings wine culture to the average person and makes it accessible and relatable. It’s people like him who open doors rather than close them, and that’s exactly the kind of attitude that earns respect from the broader community. 

Some might argue that keeping things exclusive for the upper class is intentional—I get it. There’s a certain allure in catering to a niche crowd. But in reality, a smaller vineyard selling moderately priced wines consistently over time is often far more profitable. Those ultra-rare, one-bottle sales might make headlines, but the vineyards offering quality bottles at $10 to $50 a pop are playing the long game and bringing in way more steady revenue. It’s the difference between a one-off luxury splash and sustainable, inclusive success.

- Are There Legal Requirements for Wine Labels in the United States? | Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en Are there legal requirements for wine labels in the United States? In the world of wine, labeling is a hot topic right now. With consumers becoming more aware of what they drink, understanding the legal requirements for wine labels is essential. Many people are asking what must be included on a wine label in the United States. First off, wine labels in the United States are regulated by the Alcohol and Tobacco Tax and Trade Bureau. This agency ensures that labels provide truthful information to consumers. There are several key requirements that every wine label must meet. One of the most important elements is the brand name. The label must clearly display the brand under which the wine is sold. Additionally, the class and type of wine must be identified. This could be table wine or dessert wine, for example, along with the specific variety like Chardonnay or Merlet. Another requirement is the alcohol content. The alcohol by volume must be stated, typically shown as a percentage. Consumers also need to know the net contents, which is the volume of wine in the bottle, usually indicated as 750 ml. The label must include the name and address of the bottler or importer. This helps consumers know where the wine comes from. A health warning statement about alcohol consumption is also mandatory on all wine labels. For wines from specific regions, the country or place of origin must be indicated. This is especially true for wines from American viticultural areas. If the wine contains sulfites above a certain level, the label must state that it contains sulfites. If a vintage year is mentioned, at least 85% of the wine must come from that year. For varieting, if a grape variety is named, at least 75% of the wine must be from that grape. These regulations help protect consumers by ensuring transparency about what they are drinking. The Alcohol and Tobacco Tax and Trade Bureau overseas compliance and can requiring changes to labels that do not meet these standards. State laws may also impose additional requirements or restrictions on wine labeling and sales. This means that while the federal regulations set a baseline, local laws can vary. In summary, understanding the legal requirements for wine labels is vital for both consumers and producers. With the increasing interest in wine, having accurate and clear labels is more important than ever.

- Are There Legal Limits on Offering Sample Wine in the United States? - Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en Are there legal limits on offering sample wine in the United States? Have you ever wondered about the rules surrounding wine sampling in the United States? It turns out there are specific legal limits that vary from stateto state. At the federal level, the Alcohol and Tobacco Tax and Trade Bureau regulates commercial samples of alcoholic beverages. For wine, the maximum size for a sample is for ounces per serving. This is part of a broader set of regulations that also limits beer samples to 8 o and distilled spirits samples to 2 ounces. These samples are usually exempt from internal revenue tax when they are used to solicit orders for imported products. However, there are restrictions on the number of samples that can be offered for each product within a quarter. This means that if you are a winery or retailer, you need to keep track of how many samples you provide. On top of federal regulations, each state has its own laws regarding wine tastings. Many states set their own limits on how much wine can be served per sample and per visit. For instance, some states allow micro distilleries or wineries to offer samples of about 15 milliliters, which is approximately half an ounce per beverage. The total volume permitted per visit can be around 45 ml or about 1.5 o. Additionally, states regulate who can conduct tastings. This includes licensed bars, restaurants, exclusive liquor stores, nonprofits, and charities. Each of these entities must follow specific conditions, including licensing requirements and time restrictions. Local municipalities may impose even more rules. Some places might prohibit wine sampling altogether or limit the hours during which tastings can occur. For example, certain areas may only allow tastings between 11 in the morning and 7 in the evening. These legal limits are designed to promote responsible consumption and ensure compliance with alcohol control laws. They also influence how wineries and retailers plan their tastings and marketing events. Understanding these regulations is essential for any wine related business looking to provide samples. In summary, offering sample wine in the United States is subject to federal limits of no more than 4 ounces per sample along with various state and local laws that regulate the volume and context of wine tastings. Knowing these rules can help wine businesses operate within the legal framework while providing enjoyable experiences for consumers.

- Are There Legal Requirements for Commercial Wine Storage? | Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en Are there legal requirements for commercial wine storage? Attention wine enthusiasts and business owners. A hot topic right now is the legal requirements for commercial wine storage. If you are involved in the wine industry or thinking of starting a wine storage business, you will want to know the rules. Just yesterday, discussions around compliance and regulations have gained traction as more people enter the wine market. Understanding these requirements is essential for anyone looking to store wine commercially. In the United States, commercial wine storage is governed by various legal requirements that can differ by state. First and foremost, obtaining the necessary licenses is crucial. Facilities must secure licenses to store and distribute alcoholic beverages. This includes adhering to the regulations set by the United States Treasury Department's Tax and Trade Bureau. In states like Washington, the Washington State Liquor and Cannabis Board overseas these licensing requirements. Environmental regulations are another key aspect. Wine storage facilities must manage runoff and wastewater discharge to comply with environmental laws. Pollution liability insurance is often a requirement to cover any potential environmental hazards. This is important for protecting both the business and the surrounding community. Food safety and handling guidelines also come into play. Although wine is not classified as a food product, storage facilities must follow general safety practices. This ensures that the wine remains uncontaminated and is stored properly for retail or sampling purposes. Insurance requirements cannot be overlooked. Commercial wine storage facilities typically need insurance to cover risks such as property damage and product liability. This insurance can help mitigate financial losses in the event of audits or environmental incidents. Tax compliance is yet another important factor. Facilities must adhere to tax laws, including excise taxes on wine. Accurate reporting of wine volumes is essential to avoid penalties for understated amounts. In summary, commercial wine storage involves a range of legal and regulatory requirements. These include licensing, environmental regulations, safety guidelines, insurance, and tax compliance. If you are considering entering this industry, being informed about these requirements will help you navigate the complexities of commercial wine storage. Stay informed and compliant to ensure your business thrives in this exciting market.
