- How Does Climate Change Impact Traditional Wine Regions? | Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en How does climate change impact traditional wine regions? Climate change is reshaping the world of wine, and it is a topic that has gained significant attention recently. As temperatures rise, traditional wine regions are feeling the effects more than ever. Many wine enthusiasts are curious about how these changes impact their favorite wines and the regions they come from. Let's break down the facts to understand this pressing issue. One major effect of climate change is the increase in average temperatures. This rise leads to earlier ripening of grapes, which can change the flavor profiles of wines. For example, in Oregon, wineries are adapting to hotter summers and unpredictable winters. These shifts are forcing vineyard managers to rethink their traditional practices. In Europe, countries like Italy have recorded a temperature increase of to degrees Celsius since 1980. This change affects when grapes are harvested and what varieties can thrive in these regions. In famous areas like Champagne, Bordeaux, and Burgundy, extreme cold snaps have become more common. Vineyards are now using flames to protect their crops from frost damage. The impact of climate change is not just about temperature. It also influences the types of wines that can be produced. In Spain, wineries such as Alvier are responding by creating non-fortified wines and vermouths. These options are proving to be more adaptable to the changing climate. Moreover, there is a growing interest in sustainable and eco-friendly wines. Younger consumers are increasingly prioritizing environmental responsibility. This shift is encouraging the wine industry to adopt sustainable practices. Many vineyards are turning to organic farming methods and eco-friendly packaging to meet this demand. Recent developments in the wine industry show that producers are aware of the challenges posed by climate change. They are innovating and adapting to ensure that the quality of their wines remains high. As climate change continues to affect traditional wine regions, the industry is responding with creativity and resilience. This evolution in wine- making is something to watch closely as it unfolds.

- Climate, Grapes, and Wine | Gregory Jones, Ph. D. | TEDxRoseburg: Kind: captions Language: en Transcriber: Eunice Tan Reviewer: David DeRuwe Well, welcome. Thank you very much. I'd like to start today by asking those in the audience, "Do you enjoy wine?" (Laughter) Good, good. Organizers, will you make sure that every single person with their hand up gets a free bottle of wine when we get done today? (Cheers) (Applause) Just kidding. My name's Greg Jones, as the introduction said, and I'm here to talk to you about climate, grapes, and wine. But first I'd like to back up and talk about the importance of weather and climate in human history. We know that early civilizations, as they were developing, developed based upon strong agricultural systems that had to do with the climate being able to allow them to grow a given crop. If the climate changed for one reason or another, historically, they migrated, moved somewhere else, or decided to grow something or raise a different type of animal. So climate has been very important. And today, just like in the past, we know that climate influences everything we do in agriculture - through controlling the suitability of a crop to a given area, through influencing quality and productivity, and ultimately driving economic sustainability - anywhere in the world. When we talk about climate and agriculture, we can typically talk about two broad categories: broadacre crops or specialty crops. The idea here is that these crops divide the system into how we see them geographically. While broadacre crops, such as corn, wheat, rice, and soybeans, are grown across a very wide geographic and therefore climatic range, specialty crops have a tendency to be grown over fairly narrow geographic areas and very narrow climate ranges. When I was a young PhD student starting to look at studying climate and climatology, my advisers all suggested to me to study broadacre crops because they were very important for food security. However, I was very much interested in the intricate and delicate nature of how climate influenced growing specialty crops, especially when I was looking at something like viticulture and wine production. In this system, arguably climate is the most important factor to growing the crop and making wine. So as such, I went down this path to look at this system. Some people asked me, and they said, "This is a pretty superfluous thing to study - alcohol." But alcohol has been with us throughout history and has helped shape who we are. And so I thought that was a very interesting aspect of something to study. And wine, which embodies all kinds of characteristics associated with art and history, gastronomy, romanticism, science of all kinds - it's all tied up in this one agricultural pursuit. For me, it was just too rich of a system to not study. So that's where I went down this path to become a wine climatologist, and I always say that somebody had to do it. So I want to start here by talking about something we've learned over time about this term called "terroir." Not "terror," but "terroir." "Terroir" comes from the Latin "terra" or territory. Its first modern definition is based upon a stretch of land limited by its agricultural capacity. And within the concept of terroir, it typically is trying to describe all of the factors that go into growing grapes and making wine. This includes the variety in terms of how it's being grown, but this also includes all of the natural factors, such as climate, landscape, geology, and soil, However, there's a tremendous cultural aspect too, where cultural factors are extremely important, especially in terms of how we decide what variety goes where and how those wines are being made from the fruit grown in those vineyards. So the cultural component is extremely important. Now, of all the natural factors and making wine, it is pretty clear that climate is kind of the baseline expression that we can all kind of agree on and understand, and I think most of you as well in the audience. The very baseline expression here is the difference between wine styles. We can all tell a wine that typically comes from a cool climate or a warm climate. Cool-climate wines tend to be lighter, more subtle. They have higher acidity, very crisp. They tend to have bright fruit flavors. While wines that come from a warmer terroir have a tendency to be fuller, deeper-colored, higher alcohol, and darker fruits. So we all know that this wine style framework is really being driven by climate. What happens with geology and soil is that they influence the overall subtle nature of those expressions within a given region or within a given vineyard. So it all plays an important role, but climate is the main driver here. The other component of this is that climate really is a strong driver in quality and productivity that happens within the terroir framework of things. So as I've been looking at this, terroir is an important kind of construct that we use to describe what's happening within growing grapes and making wine. [Relationships] So this leads me to talk a little bit about relationships. When we look at the overall framework of growing grapes and making wine, we know that it takes quite a bit to be able to produce a given crop for a high quality. We know we have to understand the structure of the climate in a given region, what that looks like in terms of growing season length and rainfall timing and extreme events. But we also need to know how that structure drives the suitability to any given variety grown there. But beyond structure and suitability, we also have to have a framework behind which we can understand how climate variability influences quality and productivity. But today, more than ever, we also need to understand how a changing climate influences all of this. Whether it changes the structure in a given region, the suitability to varieties, and/or the overall variability in a region is all very, very important. This is critical because wine grapes are a narrow specialty crop that are really prone to changes in climate overall. So what I've been doing is trying to study what this framework is for wine grape production, not only globally but also here, locally, within our region. So this leads me to the idea that some of you might say or be thinking about right now, the idea that "Is there an ideal climate for wine production?" Well, I've been trying to study this for about 25 years or so now, and I think we know some things that are worth sharing. Number one, I've been looking at this concept behind what's called a "climate niche." A climate niche is essentially the climatic environment that any organism, whether it be a plant or an animal, functions the best in. For wine grapes, this means if you plant the right grape in the right place, you're going to get more consistent quality and more consistent productivity over the long term. Now, as we look at overall climate structures of places worldwide, we know that climate in general is based upon what we're all led to believe - these Mediterranean climate structures out there in the world. And it's mostly true because we have this history with Europe, and we look at these areas, and we try to understand what their framework is all about. But I can tell you that wine grapes are today grown across a huge range of mid-latitude zones that are very suitable to growing quite a few different varieties. Matter of fact, hundreds of varieties can be grown across landscapes all over the world to produce different wine styles. Some of these places are more suitable to cooler-climate varieties; some of them are more suitable to warmer-climate varieties. But their overall framework here is that there's a rich amount of varietal material that can be grown in different places throughout the world in terms of what we can produce. So a lot of this has led me to studying these aspects of these climate niches and trying to better understand what variety does best where and how. And in doing so, that's led me to this idea of suitability. How can we understand suitability relative to any crop, let alone something as complex as growing grapes and making wine? I collected data from places all over the world to try to better understand this, and in doing so, I have worked up a diagram that's very useful to illustrate the concept here. This diagram is a representation of 21 of the most common varieties grown worldwide - you can pick out your favorite - and what it is is it represents the climate niche of these varieties based upon where and how they can be grown best throughout the world. I also have table grapes and raisins here just to put an upper bound on the whole framework of where the warmest areas are for grapevines. Two different things are being shown here, and we'll get to a third in a moment, but the two different things are largely the climate type in which these varieties can be grown in, whether it's cool, intermediate, warm, or hot, but also the potential wine style. And to describe that a little bit more, I want to talk about Pinot Noir. Most of you probably know Oregon's number one variety is Pinot Noir. Well, it can be grown over a fairly narrow climate niche of about four degrees Fahrenheit globally. And in doing so, on the cool end we have places like Tasmania, on the warm end we have places like the Russian River Valley, and in the middle we have places like Burgundy and the Willamette Valley. What happens here is that Pinot Noir that's grown on the cool end tends to be a lighter-bodied, lighter color, more subtle wine. While it's grown on the warmer end, it tends to be a more fuller-bodied, darker color Pinot Noir wine. So that's the wine style potential that can happen in this framework. If you really think about it, you could describe the same thing for any one of the varieties that's on this list. Another aspect of this I think is important - you might be saying, "Well, can these varieties be grown outside these bounds?" Yes, they can, and people do it. However, the climate is such that unreliable yields typically are produced, and then quality tends to be more questionable or sketchy, and so it's not a very marketable product. Within these bounds, you tend to be more consistent overall. Another way of looking at this, in terms of this diagram, is to talk about regions. I developed a concept just looking at what I call the "climatic envelope" for a given region. So this is essentially the coolest to the warmest places in a region and how it can ripen any variety. And we can talk about a couple of places. Here's Burgundy. Burgundy is a relatively cool-climate area and grows mostly Pinot Noir and Chardonnay. And you can see why - its climate envelope is basically central to that. We can also look at a warm-climate place like Bordeaux. Bordeaux, again, a little bit warmer. It can ripen a different range of varieties and also produces a different range of styles. So that brings me to talk about here. We're in the Umpqua Valley of Oregon, a very prominent wine region in the state. Where is its climate niche, and what does it look like? Well, it's basically somewhat in the middle of these two: Burgundy and Bordeaux. The framework here is - you should be able to see right away that it's wider. Well, it's wider because the region of the Umpqua Valley is larger, we also have more elevational differences and latitudinal differences. So we have a bigger climate envelope. But we do overlap: we have some characteristic climates that are very similar to Burgundy and some that are a little similar to Bordeaux as well. [Climate Change] about climate change - a lot of well-documented changes that have happened out there in terms of climate and climate-controlled factors in wine regions. We know that growing seasons have gotten warmer and longer. We know that winter periods have been warmer as well. The plants are telling us that things are changing because they have earlier budbreak and flowering and harvest. We also know that ripening characteristics or profiles have changed. We've been accumulating more sugar and producing slightly higher-alcohol wines, and therefore wine styles have changed because of it. Pests and diseases have changed. We've also seen some changes overall in characteristics of soil erosion and fertility. And we've also seen water resource changes, where some places have gotten drier and some places have gotten wetter. But let's look at a very basic change - getting back to this diagram that we've just been talking about and put it into a framework of trying to understand how changes in climate influence not only how varieties can be produced but how regions can produce those varieties based upon their climate envelope. I'm going to go back to Burgundy. Burgundy in the 1950s was relatively cool - cooler than it is today - and it was still able to produce Pinot Noir and Chardonnay, its two marquee varieties, but did it in a slightly different wine style framework. If you of course fast-forward to where we are today, you can see that that shift of a couple of degrees is more central to those two varieties and so therefore allows it to be produced more consistently. If we look at conservative estimates of warming in the future, Burgundy will likely end up here. The climatic envelope will be on the warm upper end for Pinot Noir and Chardonnay. It doesn't remove them from the ability to produce those wines, but what it does is it changes the wine style overall framework that they can produce there. If we look at this, though, this is a conservative projection of climate in the future. What happens if it changes to more extremes than we kind of tend to think might happen? This would put Burgundy in a completely different framework, where only the coolest of places can potentially ripen fruit in the future, and potentially there will be other varieties that can be done there. How about if we go to where we are in the Umpqua Valley and think about it? We go back to the 1950s in the Umpqua Valley; the climates were a little cooler than they are today. The first pioneers that came here in the 1960s planted varieties that were more associated with that cooler climate and did fairly well. Fast-forward to today, this is our climate envelope. We still produce some of those cool-climate varieties, but now we've shifted a little bit, and we're producing other varieties. How about conservative warming in the growing seasons of four to five degrees Fahrenheit by 2050? This is what our climate envelope would be like. We would be shifting a little bit in terms of what we can do, but we'd also be opening up potentially to new varieties in terms of our overall productivity capability. [The Future] So that leads me to the idea of "What does the future hold?" Well, first, we know that today, the climate is warmer than at any time in our recorded past. We also know that continued warming is highly likely. While climate change has the ability to affect every single agricultural pursuit, the narrow zones of wine grape production are especially prone to climate change. And so I've provided us some good evidence of kind of these type of impacts into a specialty crop system. So as we look forward to the future, we have to think a little bit about kind of what this means for the overall perspective of wine-grape growing. We know that the past 50 years have benefited quality of wine worldwide with some climate change. But our projections into the future have a tendency to point to the idea that some beneficial impacts will happen, but we'll likely have some detrimental impacts as well. I personally believe that Oregon has plenty to gain from overall climate change, and I think that we'll see some of that. Our warming in our cool to intermediate climates will likely produce a situation in which we have increased viability for some varieties, but we can also potentially see new varieties that we can grow. However, our warm and dry summers are projected to get nothing more than warmer and drier. And what that might do is that might increase heat stress and/or increase issues of water resource demand. So I think we have some real important issues there. I have a lot of confidence in viticulture and wine production to address these issues. They already have in increasing their sustainability and are constantly looking to reduce their vulnerability and increase their adaptive capacity in the face of climate change. They're doing this through looking at better plant material, how to better manage vineyards, but also how to take that fruit from the vineyard into the winery and control quality into the future. All of that being said, I think all of us as wine consumers need to be adaptable to change as well. Change is going to happen, whether it be changes in wine styles or wine varieties. This is just bound to happen in all the wine regions in the world. If you are a true "terroir-ist" and you love wine, then embrace these changes. Go out there and continually try to find the best wine you can find from the magic that is terroir. Thank you.

- How Do Climates Affect Wine Regions Around the World? - Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en How do climates affect wine regions around the world? Imagine walking through a vineyard and tasting grapes that have grown in a specific climate. Every sip of wine from that vineyard tells a story about its weather and environment. Climate is a key factor in shaping where wine can be made and what kind of wine it will be. Temperature is one of the biggest influences. Warmer climates speed up grape ripening, leading to higher sugar levels and potentially higher alcohol content. Cooler areas slow down ripening, which helps grapes develop more acidity and complex flavors. Precipitation and humidity also matter a lot. Too much rain can cause grapes to swell and dilute flavors, and it can lead to fungal diseases. Too little rain stresses the vines, which can concentrate flavors, but also reduce yields. Extreme weather events like hail storms, floods, and wildfires are becoming more common and can damage vineyards directly. For example, hail can ruin a whole season's harvest, and droughts can cause vines to struggle, lowering grape quality. Climate change is shifting the traditional boundaries of wine regions. Places like southern Europe and parts of Australia are experiencing more heat waves and droughts, making some areas less suitable for growing grapes. Meanwhile, cooler regions like northern France, the United Kingdom, and parts of Canada are becoming more promising for wine production. These areas are now able to grow grapes that previously wouldn't ripen well. Wine makers are adjusting by choosing different grape varieties and changing how they manage their vineyards. They might plant more heat tolerant grapes or use different rootstocks to help vines cope with stress. But these adaptations have limits and some regions may face economic challenges if the climate continues to change rapidly. Extreme weather events are also causing more damage. Wildfires in California and Australia have destroyed vineyards and floods in Europe have washed away crops. All these factors show how climate directly influences where and how wine is made. As the climate shifts, the map of wine regions is changing too, with some areas becoming less suitable and others opening new opportunities for wine makers. Understanding these climatic effects helps producers plan for the future and keep making great wines. [Music]

I started to sleep through the night again, though the security camera ping still startled me. I even hosted a small charity tasting in my cellar. 15 guests, five wines, $5,000 raised for a local food pantry. Every pour, every toast was my middle finger to the idea that passion equals pathology. Then one Thursday afternoon, my phone lit up. Mom. I almost declined it, but something, curiosity or masochism, made me answer. Her voice was soft, wet with practiced remorse. Avery, honey, I miss you. We all do. I've been thinking about how wrong we were. Could we please have dinner? Just to talk. No ambushes, no cameras. I promise. I said nothing for several seconds. Behind her, I could hear plates clinking. Domestic comforting sounds that once meant home. Then Dad's voice came on, gentler than I'd heard in months. We've been seeing a counselor. We handled things terribly. Please just come. Saturday night, just family. When he said just family, something inside me flinched. But hope is a stubborn animal. It never dies. It just changes costume. I agreed. 1 hour, dinner only. If anyone besides you two is there, I said, I leave immediately. They swore on everything holy. Saturday evening, 6 and 12 p.m. I drove up their street in halflight, the kind of suburban dusk where lawns glow green and windows flicker with TV blue. There were more cars than there should have been. Monica's truck, Patrick's minivan, Auntie's Prius, Uncle Robert's sedan. Four extra cars I didn't recognize. I texted Ethan. Too many cars. You might be right. He called instantly. Leave. Don't even go in. I'll record everything," I said. He swore under his breath. "Please, just be careful." The second I walked in, I knew the air had weight. Conversations died mid-sentence. 25 people in the living room, folding chairs arranged in a half circle facing the couch. At the center, a woman in a navy blazer holding a leather folder labeled Avery's Intervention. My mother stood at her side, trembling, but not with surprise. My father sat rigid, staring at the floor. Monica's eyes glowed, vindication disguised as concern. "Good evening," said the woman, stepping forward. "I'm Dr. Karen much and wants to help you reclaim your life." The words slid off her tongue like a script she'd performed hundreds of times. "I felt the phone in my pocket and pressed record." "Of course you're recording," Monica muttered, arms folded. Dr. Matthews smiled, unbothered. That's fine. This is a safe space. She gestured for me to sit. I didn't. One by one, they began reading from prepared papers. Monica first. Avery, your addiction has changed you. You used to be my best friend. Now you're paranoid, defensive, alone. You called the police on family. You're sick and can't see it. Patrick, next. The kids miss you. They ask why Aunt Avery loves her bottles more than them. Mom, voice breaking. I wake up every night terrified you'll drink yourself to death. I've already picked what I'll wear to your funeral. Dad, you're not the daughter I raised. The collection owns you now. Even Aunt Denise chimed in. Remember at Christmas 2019 you talked about wine for 20 minutes. That was the first sign. Then a final blow. Lena. She was sitting quietly near the window. When our eyes met, she looked regretful. They called me, she said softly. I didn't know they'd do this. I just said you drink responsibly. but they twisted it. Dr. Matthews raised her hand. Thank you, everyone. Avery, this isn't about judgment. It's about safety. She opened her folder and pulled out several documents. This, she said, is the treatment plan. It includes a 90-day inpatient program, property management during your absence, and financial guardianship to ensure stability. Monica stepped forward holding a thick packet, property transfer agreement. You sign the house to me for safekeeping, she said. It's temporary. After a year sober, we can revisit ownership. I stared at the papers. My name was already printed on every line. Someone had practiced my signature. You tried to steal my house, I said. Dr. Matthews interjected smoothly. Denial often expresses as hostility. It's normal, Monica reached for my hand. Please, Avery, don't make us do this the hard way. That was when Uncle Robert moved toward the door, positioning himself like a guard. "Sit down," he said softly. "Just listen." I pulled out my phone, thumbmed 911. Robert grabbed my arm. I looked at him, not furious, not scared, just done. "Let go," I said. "Now." Dr. Matthews tried to mediate. "Everyone calm. Let me go," I repeated. He did. The entire room froze. I backed toward the door. Mom sobbed. "Don't walk away, please. We can save you. Monica shouted. You'll regret this when you hit rock bottom. Dr. Matthews, ever calm, said, "Let her process. Rock bottom looks different for everyone." I opened the door, turned back once. "Rock Bottom," I said, "was trusting any of you." Then I left. Outside, the night was cold enough to sting. I sat in my car, shaking, not from fear, from disbelief that flesh and blood people could plot something so intricate under the flag of love. My phone buzzed with Ethan's text. You okay? On my way. When he arrived, I was still in the driver's seat. Engine off. He opened my door gently. "You recorded it?" I nodded. He pulled me into his arms and said nothing for a long time. Just held me like the silence could be a shield. By Monday, it was online. A Facebook page titled Prayers for Avery's Recovery. Old photos, captions like before the addiction took her. extended family commenting hearts and verses. Worse, someone had posted my police report from the break-in. Monica's caption, "She called the cops on family who were just trying to help." My inbox flooded with messages from colleagues, clients, even an old college professor. Are you okay? Do you need support? Is the story true? Sharon, my boss, called me into her office again. She was kind but careful. the way companies are when reputation might be contagious. I explained everything, showed her footage, documents, my drinking log. She sighed. Legally, you're fine. Emotionally, I'll get therapy, I said. That night, I emailed an attorney specializing in defamation and family fraud. Subject line: Need help before they ruin my life completely. He replied within an hour, "You're not crazy. They're building a paper trail. Let's start building yours." Days later, Dr. Matthews sent me an email herself. "Your family's courage is inspiring. When you're ready to accept treatment, I'll be here." I replied once. "I consume less than the CDC's definition of moderate drinking. What you staged was attempted theft." Her response came 5 minutes later. "Denial with statistical manipulation is classic high functioning alcoholism." Heartbreaking. I closed my laptop and laughed, a sharp, exhausted sound that echoed through the empty house. Then I poured myself one glass of wine, not to drown anything, but to reclaim what was mine. The cellar light glowed gold against stone. Every bottle around me was quiet, loyal, patient, everything my family wasn't. Still, I knew this wasn't over. Because people who mistake control for care don't stop when you say no. They stop when they're forced to. And I had a feeling I'd find out just how far love could go when money was involved. 6 weeks. That's how long the quiet lasted. Long enough for me to start believing the storm had passed or at least moved on to someone else's house. I went back to work full-time, started therapy, even smiled when Ethan joked that I should rename the cellar the fortress. I tried, really tried to believe it was over. Then on a gray Tuesday morning, my phone rang. Christopher, my neighbor. Avery, you home? No, I'm at the office. Why? There's a forale sign in your yard. For a second, I thought he was kidding. He wasn't. And uh there are people walking through your living room. My heart stopped. I hung up and drove like gravity didn't apply to me. 30 minutes later, I pulled up to my street and there it was. Century 21 for sale. Open house today. A lock box on my front door. Strangers strolling around my porch like it belonged to them. Inside, a realtor with a neat bun and clipboard looked startled to see me. Oh, you must be Mr. Hale, I'm Ms. Caldwell, I said the owner. What's happening here? She blinked, then checked her notes. Oh. Oh, dear. Your brother said you were in a rehab program. I'm so sorry. He signed all the documents. She showed me the listing agreement. It had my name and my forged signature right next to temporary guardian Monica Hail. It was almost perfect. Almost. The address on the rehab facility paperwork was real, but the letterhead wasn't. They'd built an entire narrative right down to a fake intake date and a counselor's signature. Dr. Karen Matthews. I felt something in me break so cleanly it made no sound. And then I heard him. Monica's husband Patrick standing in the kitchen talking to a young couple. We're planning to renovate the basement, make it a playroom. The seller used it as wine storage, but that'll all be cleared out. The seller? He was calling me the seller. When he saw me, he didn't flinch. Oh, you're back early, he said flatly. How was treatment? I pulled out my phone and started filming. You're done, I said. All of you, he rolled his eyes. You're making a scene. The realtor looked terrified now. Should I Should I call the office? Yes, I said. And while you're at it, call the police. The officers arrived quickly. Same precinct, same older cop from before. When I explained, he frowned. You filed a restraining order, right? Yes. Then this is fraud on top of trespass. They took photos of everything. The sign, the forged paperwork, the lock box drilled into my doorframe. While they worked, I stood in my own front yard, shaking, watching potential buyers hurry back to their cars. Ethan arrived mid chaos, winded from speeding. "Tell me they didn't. They tried," I said again. He didn't answer, just took my hand. The investigation unfolded faster than I expected. Maybe because this time I had every receipt, every recording, every time stamp. Police obtained a warrant and searched Monica's house. What they found was a grotesque kind of ambition, practice sheets of my signature, blueprints for our new home, my house, annotated with remove cellar racks, kids playroom, Ethan's office, guest bedroom, emails with Dr. Matthews arranging the fake rehab documentation. Text threads with my parents discussing the transition timeline. My stomach turned when I saw a folder labeled Caldwell liquidation plan. They'd itemized everything, my furniture, my art, even my car. Monica had already quit one of her jobs. Patrick had applied for a home equity line of credit on my address. And my parents, they had called my HR department at least six times, reporting concern for Avery's mental fitness. There was a file documenting every call. They weren't just trying to steal my house. They were trying to erase me from it. The deeper the detectives dug, the worse it got. They found forged bank statements, loan applications, altered investment documents, a complete fake medical file with admission records signed by Dr. Matthews. Apparently, she'd told the judge in another county that I was institutionalized and therefore unable to manage personal affairs. A judge had granted temporary conservatorship to Monica. My parents had witnessed it. If Christopher hadn't called me about the for sale sign, the sale would have gone through. By the time I noticed, they would have owned everything. The trial came fast. My lawyer said the evidence was overwhelming. The prosecutor laid it out like a true crime documentary. One, create a false narrative of addiction. Two, establish a fake paper trail of instability. Three, obtain conservatorship through fraud. Four, sell Avery's assets. Five, transfer proceeds to Monica. All of it for a house worth maybe $450,000 and a wine collection worth another h 100,000. When the judge read the charges, Monica looked defiant. Patrick looked scared. My parents just looked small. Dr. Matthews lost her license before the sentencing even began. The notary who authenticated the forged documents was arrested too. In court, Monica said, "We were trying to save her." The judge didn't even blink. You created an elaborate fiction to steal from your sister. That isn't intervention. That's conspiracy. 18 months in prison for Monica, 5 years probation for Patrick, probation and fines for my parents, community service for the notary, and restitution, almost $80,000 for me. It didn't feel like victory. It felt like burial. When the verdict was read, my mother wailed. We've lost both our daughters. I didn't correct her. The woman she used to call her daughter no longer existed. They killed her when they decided her success was evidence of sin. After court, Ethan and I walked out into the sunlight. He asked quietly, "You okay?" "No," I said, "but I'm free." We stood in the parking lot for a long time, not speaking. Then he said, "You should open that Marggo tonight." I think I will. That night, I unccorked the 1982 Chateau Margo they'd tried to sell. The cork came out clean. No crumble, no resistance. The scent hit like memory and defiance all at once. I poured one glass, looked at my cellar walls, and whispered, "You survived. So did I." I raised my glass to the cameras, the court files, the neighbor who called, and the truth that had refused to stay buried. The seller hummed quietly, a reminder that peace doesn't mean quiet. It means no one else has a key. Freedom didn't arrive as fireworks. It came as silence. The kind that finally feels earned. 6 months after the trial, I stood in my cellar again, barefoot, hair still damp from the rain outside. The bottles glowed softly under the new lighting I'd installed. Golden halos reflected off glass shoulders. For the first time in a year, I could breathe in here without flinching. Every shelf was where it belonged. Every label faced forward, and every sound, from the low hum of the climate system to the soft click of the door ceiling shut, was mine. The restitution money came in two installments. My lawyer called it a symbolic gesture. I called it funding for new locks and a therapist who doesn't flinch when I mention family. I used part of it to rebuild the tasting counter Monica had smashed during the break-in. The granite gleamed again, cooler than forgiveness. And beside it, I framed a simple line on the wall. Blood may be thicker than wine, but wine doesn't try to steal your house. Ethan thought it was darkly funny. I thought it was the truest thing I'd ever written. My parents move three towns over after sentencing. They send letters sometimes, all written in the same trembling tone of false peace. We forgive you. Family is forever. When you're ready to come home, our door is open. Every word is dipped in nostalgia and denial. I don't reply. I don't need to. Some stories end not with closure, but with distance. Monica writes from prison, too. Long, handwritten pages full of excuses and self-pity. You have no idea how hard things were for us. We did it for your own good. You'll understand someday. I've learned to let her words burn themselves out in the mailbox. Sometimes Ethan reads them first, just to spare me. Sometimes we make a joke out of it. New episode of family crime drama just dropped. But most days I just recycle them unopened. Paper doesn't deserve to keep that kind of power. Ethan and I are still together. He doesn't talk about what happened unless I do. And he never treats me like a fragile thing. The first time he came over after the sentencing, he stood in front of the cellar door for a long moment. You keeping it locked? He asked. Always. Good, he said. But open it anyway. Let's make new memories in there. So we did. We opened the 1982 Margo. The bottle Monica had tried to sell for cash. The cork released with a sigh, like the seller, exhaling after holding its breath too long. We poured two glasses and stood side by side under the amber light. It was rich, smoky, velvet soft, everything I'd dreamed it would be when I first bought it. We didn't talk much. Some wines deserve silence. A few months later, I completed my level three sumeier certification. My instructor congratulated me with a grin. You've got an insane pallet, Avery. You should teach. I laughed. I already did, I said. Just not in a classroom. Because in a twisted way, my family taught me everything about tasting life. The bitterness of betrayal, the dryness of disbelief, the surprising sweetness of survival. The house feels like mine again, but it's quieter now. Not in a lonely way, just settled. The laughter I hear comes from people who actually know me, Ethan's friends, a few co-workers, even Lena, who reached out after testifying in court. She told the judge I was the most responsible drinker she'd ever met. Afterward, she apologized for ever picking up my mother's call. It's okay, I told her. They fooled me longer than they fooled you. Now sometimes she joins our tastings and the three of us laugh over flights of Cabernet and second chances. Therapy helped not to forgive them. That word still tastes sour, but to stop bleeding energy into their absence. My therapist said something that stuck. Sometimes justice is the quiet after the storm, not the apology that never comes. She was right. Justice wasn't the verdict. It was the fact that I could walk into my cellar, pour a glass, and not feel like a defendant in my own home. Every month on the date the trial ended, I open one bottle from the section Monica almost emptied. Sometimes I share it with friends. Sometimes I drink alone with music low and the window open. Each sip feels like defiance, but also gratitude. Proof that I survive something meant to erase me. My collection now stands at 850 bottles. Each cataloged, insured, and entirely mine. Not because I'm obsessive, but because it's how I honor what they tried to steal. my autonomy. People ask sometimes if I'll ever speak to my family again. I tell them the truth. Maybe someday if they ever learn the difference between help and control, but I doubt it. Some people only feel whole when they're cutting pieces out of you. So I build my own version of family. Chosen, patient, kind, the ones who don't need my downfall to prove their love. And when we toast, I always raise my glass to the same thing. To peace, to proof, to never doubting your own story again. The seller hums softly in agreement, its air cool and clean. I take a sip. It tastes like oak and earth and something rarer.

The lawyer sent the demand letter 2 days later. It was short, clinical, brutal in the way only legal language can be. It outlined the facts, cited theft and unlawful entry, demanded the immediate return of all funds from the sale of my property, and warned that failure to complain would result in both civil action and a police report. I didn't tell my parents it was coming. I didn't owe them that courtesy anymore. They received it on a Friday morning. I know because my phone started blowing up before noon. My mother left seven voicemails in under an hour. The first was tearful. The second was confused. By the fourth, the tears had dried up and something sharp had replaced them. By the seventh, she was furious. "How could you do this to us?" she demanded. "After everything we've done for you, my father didn't call." He sent a single text. "This is a mistake. You're embarrassing the family." That line told me exactly where his priorities were. Not legality, not boundaries, not the fact that they'd broken into my home and sold thousands, no, tens of thousands of dollars worth of my property. What mattered was that I'd stopped playing my assigned role. Within hours, the family narrative shifted hard. Suddenly, I wasn't just an alcoholic. I was unstable, ungrateful. My aunt posted a vague Facebook status about when helping someone turns into betrayal. My cousin messaged me to say I was weaponizing the law against my own parents. My brother called and asked if I'd lost my mind. That one hurt the most. I asked him quietly if he thought it was okay that they broke into my house. There was a long pause. Well, no, he said, but you didn't have to escalate it like this. Escalate? They stole from me. I protected myself. And somehow I was the one who went too far. The next day, my parents' pastor called. I hadn't spoken to him in years. He left a message about forgiveness, about grace, about how families sometimes make hard choices out of love. I deleted it without responding. The idea that they'd pulled religious authority into this made my stomach turn. Then came the real threat. My mother texted me late that night. If you keep pushing this, we'll tell the authorities you're a danger to yourself. I stared at my phone for a long time. That wasn't concern. That was coercion. I forwarded the message to my lawyer. He responded immediately. Do not engage. Save everything. The following week was a blur of tension and silence. My parents went quiet, which somehow felt worse. No calls, no texts, no passive aggressive updates through relatives. Just nothing. I imagined them scrambling, trying to figure out how to regain control without exposing themselves further. Then the money hit my account. Not all of it, but most of it. No apology, no explanation, just a lumpsum transfer labeled return of funds. The missing amount, several thousand, was conspicuously absent. My lawyer wasn't surprised. He said it was likely claimed as expenses, storage, transport fees, as if their crime came with a service charge. Legally, I could have pushed harder, taken it to court, dragged it out, exposed everything. Part of me wanted to, but another part of me was exhausted. I had my proof. I had my money and I had something far more important than revenge clarity. I told my lawyer to close the case unless they interfered again. That decision didn't bring peace. It brought fallout. My parents told the family they'd returned the money to keep the situation from getting uglier. Not because they were wrong, because I was unreasonable. According to them, I'd chosen money over family. I'd threatened them. I'd humiliated them. I'd refused help. And the family believed them. Invitations stopped coming. Group chats went quiet when I joined. At a birthday party I did attend. Conversations stalled when I entered the room, then resumed in hush tones once I left. I wasn't confronted anymore. I was avoided, treated like a liability. The label stuck. Even months later, people still asked if I was doing better, if I was back on track, if I'd cut back. No amount of truth could undo the story they preferred. What broke me wasn't the isolation. It was the realization that none of this had actually been about wine. If it hadn't been bottles, it would have been something else. My job, my lifestyle, my independence. The moment I stopped needing my parents approval, they'd found a way to frame that as pathology. Because control, disguised as concern is still control. I sold the house a few months later. Not because I had to, but because I wanted distance, physical, emotional, symbolic. I rebuilt a smaller collection quietly, selectively, without sharing it with anyone who thought ownership equaled addiction. I don't talk to my parents anymore. Not because I'm angry, though some days I still am, but because trust, once broken that deeply, doesn't regenerate. They still tell people they tried to save me. And maybe that's what hurts the most. That in their version of the story, they're heroes. And I'm the cautionary tale. But here's the truth I've learned the hard way. Anyone willing to steal your autonomy to protect, you will always believe they're right. And the moment you stop letting them, they'll call you the problem loudly so no one notices what they did. The silence that followed was louder than any argument we'd ever had. No more fantic calls, no more fake concern, no more ultimatums dressed up as love. Just a clean, empty absence where my parents used to be. And for the first time in my life, I could think without their voices echoing in my head. At first, I told myself the distance was temporary, that eventually someone would cool down, admit things went too far, and reach out like an adult. Weeks passed. Then months, the apologies never came. What did come were secondhand messages filtered through relatives who still believed they were being neutral. They miss you. You know, they're hurt. You shut them out. They did what they thought was best. That last one always made my jaw tighten. Doing what you think is best doesn't excuse stripping someone of their agency. It doesn't excuse breaking into their home. And it certainly doesn't excuse trying to force them into a medical gram to prove a point. I stopped responding to those messages, not out of spite, but out of self-preservation. What surprised me was how much lighter I felt once I stopped trying to explain myself. I didn't need to convince anyone I wasn't an alcoholic. I didn't need to defend my hobbies, my finances, or my adulthood. The people who mattered had already figured it out on their own. A few friends admitted something that stuck with me. They said they'd noticed my parents behavior long before I had. The subtle monitoring, the guilt, laced advice, the way every success came with strings attached. One friend put it perfectly. They don't see you as a person. They see you as an extension. That realization hurt, but it also clarified everything. Over time, I rebuilt my life on my own terms. I found new routines, new interests. I started hosting small dinners for people who didn't flinch at a wine rack or count glasses. I kept my collection modest, intentional, and private. Not because I was ashamed, but because I'd learned that some parts of my life deserve protection. Therapy helped, too. Not because I needed rehab, but because I needed to unlearn the idea that love comes with surveillance. My therapist didn't once focus on alcohol. She focused on boundaries, on inshment, on how easily concern can become control when people are afraid of losing relevance. The hardest part wasn't losing my parents. It was accepting that they might never change. That they may go to their graves believing they were right. That I was sick, stubborn, or saved from something worse by their actions. letting go of the hope that they'd one day understand was its own kind of grief. But grief, I learned, doesn't mean regret. I don't regret standing up for myself. I don't regret involving a lawyer. I don't regret choosing peace over proximity. What I regret is how long it took me to believe that my adulthood didn't need their permission. Every so often, I hear updates. My mother still tells people she worries about me. my father's still story as a wake up call that I refuse to hear and maybe that's the version they need to live with themselves. I'm fine with mine because my version includes a quiet house, locked doors, and the knowledge that no one has the right to decide who I am without my consent. It includes a life where hobbies are joy, not evidence, and where love isn't conditional on compliance. If there's one thing I tell anyone reading this, it's this. If someone needs to take something from you to help you, they're not helping. They're asserting ownership. And the moment you assert yourself back, they'll call you ungrateful and stable, broken. Let them. Because sometimes the healthiest thing you can do isn't proving you're okay. It's walking away from the people who need you to be unwell to justify their control.

- Why Is Humidity Important for Wine Storage and Aging? - Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en Why is humidity important for wine storage and aging? Imagine you have a bottle of fine wine and you want to ensure it ages beautifully. One key factor in achieving that is humidity. Humidity plays a vital role in preserving wine quality during storage and aging. It primarily helps maintain the integrity of the cork, which acts as the wine's seal against air exposure. Optimal humidity levels for wine storage are generally between 60% and 70%. This range keeps corks moist and flexible. When corks dry out, they lose their elasticity and can shrink. This creates tiny gaps that allow air to enter the bottle. When air gets in, oxidation occurs, which can spoil the wine's flavor. It can lead to premature aging and undesirable tastes like bitterness or acidity. On the other hand, if humidity levels rise above 75%, corks can become overly saturated and weak. This makes them prone to breaking apart when you try to open the bottle. High humidity can also encourage mold growth on corks and labels. While mold rarely affects the wine directly, it can damage the bottle's appearance and reduce its value, especially for collectors. Mold and mildew can create unhealthy storage conditions and cause labels to peel or smudge, complicating identification and diminishing aesthetic appeal. Humidity also influences evaporation rates inside wine storage environments. Proper humidity levels minimize evaporation through the cork. This helps preserve the wine's balance of flavors and alcohol content over time. If humidity is too low, evaporation increases, concentrating the wine unevenly, and altering its intended profile. Maintaining the right humidity is essential for protecting the wine's chemical composition and preserving the bottle's physical condition. Many wine storage solutions include humidifiers and monitoring systems to keep humidity within the ideal range. This ensures that wine ages gracefully and retains its character and value. In summary, humidity is important for wine storage and aging because it keeps corks moist and elastic, prevents oxidation by limiting air ingress, controls evaporation to maintain wine balance, discourages mold growth that can damage corks and labels, and protects the bottle's appearance and resale value. Proper humidity management along with temperature control creates an environment where wine can develop its flavors as intended over time. [Music]

And so all of those inherent costs of the land are passed on to you, the consumer. if you look at Old Word Wines, the lands in the vineyards have already been paid for 10 times over. If you, you know, bought a wine, a $50 bottle of wine from the United States and a $50 bottle of wine from France and kind of felt like, wow, you've got more value for your money out of the European wine, inherent land costs is probably one of those factors in that. Most vineyards don't produce any fruit that you can actually make into wine until what we call third or fourth leaf. So that's three or four years that you have to wait that you can't make any wine in yet. You don't get any profit from. So now that we're on our third or fourth leaf, now it's time to harvest the fruit so we can make wine. If you use a machine, generally you're using it to harvest a large vineyard, right? And so it's definitely economical that way and costs less money. If you're hand harvesting it's in areas that you really can't get into, you know, hillsides all of those different kinds of things you get a better selection but it takes a team of people which is more labor intensive. And that is also passed So another factor when you plant and harvest your vineyard is how many tons per acre you're gonna produce. So if you produce a lot of tons per acre, seven eight, nine, 10, you're gonna get more fruit, but less quality fruit with less concentration. Not a lot of nuance. If you're doing one and a half acre you're gonna get a better developed wine with also a lot more flavor and depth. Definitely if we're looking at California Cabernet, you know, under $20, generally, it's probably harvested at a pretty large ton per acre. One and a half is kind of the standard when you're looking at like some of the top vineyards that you're gonna pay the price for. So we're gonna move on to vintage. Vintage is just the year that the grapes were harvested. There's non vintage wines, but these are vintage wines. So these are vintage stated. We've kind of already moved a step up. These are vintage stated wines, which is a sign of quality. A good vintage simply means that they were the prime growing conditions to produce ripe fruit, to make grape wine. You were up against mother nature and it's constantly throwing curve balls at you. Weather, rain. I think one year that we had a harvest in 2010 we actually referred to that as the Albert Hitchcock Vintage. 30% of the crop was lost due to the birds coming in and eating a lot of the fruit. You know, and vintage is important, but I think for your everyday drinking wines that becomes less important. You know, so if you're looking at something like these two wines here, you're gonna consume these wines, have fun with them. As we start to move up in price point, we look at, you know, these couple of wines here a lot of people use these as investments. They're gonna appreciate value. If you're really into wine and collecting wine, then vintage matters, right? And especially as you start to make more money, a lot of the more details is the same thing. The nuances and detail, you pay more attention to when you start to spend a lot more money. 2018 is considered a good vintage in California. When we talk about a good vintage, it's not only the growing conditions, it's also about longevity. Perfect growing conditions for someplace like California means that we are gonna make these long live wines that actually don't start giving until 20 or 30 years after they've been made. Especially when you're starting to listen to wine critics. At an everyday drinking level, it's more about pleasure and consuming those wines. And you need to pay less attention to vintage. I always have a rule of thumb, and great vintages you wanna buy from everybody because it was hard to make bad wine that year. And bad vintages you wanna buy from the best because they show a track record of making great wine consistently throughout the years. How do you know if it's a good vintage or not? By reading periodicals and what other people say, talking to the winery and those things you can figure out whether it's a good year or not at a certain price point every year is the best year to drink to wine. So now we're gonna move on to marketing and packaging. So we're actually gonna look at the raw materials. So glass, foil, labels and all those things and the designs and stuff like that. I would have to say that this is probably not a major factor on the pricing. As we're kind of looking at like all the marketing for these four bottles here, you know, there's a vast difference in price, but from looking at everything collectively, there's not a vast difference in the amount that was spent on packaging. The consumer would never know, but the idea from a winery side, if you're spending more than $8 on packaging, that's considered expensive. So that would encompass everything. The glass is probably your major cost here. We truly live in a day and age now where you can't really judge a wine by its label. You know, they have really great labels and fun labels and kitschy labels that actually really have great wine inside. I think a lot of times when we use that term marketing, we feel like we're being duped or tricked. Everything that you do is marketing, even if you're anti-marketing. As we look at something like this, you know this could be the $300 bottle of wine in the day and age that we live in now. I think, you know, maybe 20 years ago, this would just be unheard of. And most people would bypass that and look at something a little bit more serious but those days are over. You know, there's a lot of great wine in interesting packaging. So the last factor we're gonna talk about is reputation, which I think for most people doesn't really come to mind but it definitely plays a role in the price. You look at some of these higher end wines, you know, Mayacamas at 135, Diamond Creek, you know, they've been making wine for years. They've kind of set the standard for American wine making. They are at the very tip top of what the best we have to offer from the United States. You know what you're gonna get, you know what type of quality you're gonna get. And thus that's incorporated in the price as well. Versus some of the newer guys starting out, they can't demand the same kind of prices even if they were doing the same quality of stuff because there's not that reputation there. So they're still developing trust. Whereas here they have over 50 years experience, if not more, of making some of the great wines in America. Here, you can go right on their website. These are allocated, you sign up for the mailing list and they assign how much wine they wanna sell you. So no matter what kind of vintage it is a lot of people are still lined up and they still wanna buy these wines based upon that trust and reputation. Yeah, so if we're looking at these four particular wines I think the biggest factors, if I was gonna rate them would be region. That's a big one. Vineyard. Then we'd move on to the aging and barrel storage. And then from there reputation and then marketing. I'm sure there's cynics out there, people out there to say, "Oh, this is $18. And this is $300. And you know, the reason why that is that is because it's marketing." That's just not the case in this particular instance. There's definitely things that they do that make this cost more. There's a reason why these wines are priced the way that they are. And the biggest factor in that is that you can taste the difference. It's crystal clear that the wines are made differently. They taste differently and what it takes to make this wine versus to make this wine is definitely reflected in the price.

Labor theory of value vs. Subjective theory of value. Even if the European land has been paid off and the American land is $1m per acre, both are ultimately irrelevant to the price of the wine.

The price of the wine is what consumers are willing to pay for it, and that determines whether it is profitable for those vineyards to continue operations. So if the europeans have lower cost of land, they can operate much more profitably all things being equal. - Wine Law and Pricing Explained for WSET 3: Kind: captions Language: en Welcome to the explainer. Today we are unccorting two of the most important topics in wine. And if you're studying for something like the WCT level 3, this is absolutely for you. We're going to get to the bottom of why one bottle is 10 bucks and another is a thousand. And we'll decode all that legal jargon on the label so you know what it really means. All right, so let's just jump right in with the big question, right? You're standing in a wine shop looking at a wall of bottles. Why does one cost less than a sandwich and another costs more than a plane ticket? Well, I promise you it's not just fancy marketing. It's a really fascinating mix of costs from the vineyard all the way to the shelf and a whole world of regulations. So, here's how we're going to break it all down. First up, we'll look at the costs from the grape to the glass. Then, we'll follow the wine on its journey to the shelf. After that, we're going to get into the law on the label, specifically how it works in Europe. And finally, we'll put it all together and see exactly how price meets law. Okay, section one, from grape to glass. You see, the price of a bottle of wine starts building long before it's even wine. It really all begins out there in the vineyard with the land, the vines, and just how much work it takes to grow great grapes. You know, this right here shows you just how much the land itself can drive up the cost. If you've got flat vineyards, you can bring in machines to do almost everything, which keeps costs way down. But on some of those world famous steep slopes, like in the Mosul in Germany, no way. It's all done by hand. I mean, sometimes the vineyards are so steep that workers literally have to be tied on with ropes. That kind of manual labor is incredibly expensive. And you better believe that cost gets passed right into the bottle. And once those grapes finally get to the winery, the costs just keep piling up. I mean, first there's the huge expense of just setting up the vineyard and buying all the equipment. We're talking tanks, presses, the whole shebang. But then there's the cost of time itself, maturation. Think about it. If a wine maker is aging a wine in an oak barrel for 2 or 3 years, that wine is just sitting there taking up space and not making a single penny. That delay, that tied up cash has to be factored into the final price. All right, so the wine is made, it's been aged, we're done, right? Not even close. Getting that finished wine from the seller to your local shop adds a whole other set of costs to the equation. Yeah, this map of the supply chain really shows it all. Every single step here adds a little bit more to that final price tag. Take packaging. A super heavy, fancy, custom molded bottle costs a lot more to make and ship than a simple lightweight one. Then there's transport. Shipping wine across the globe costs a fortune compared to just driving it down the road. And of course, the importer, the distributor, the retailer, they all need to get their cut along the way. Oh, and let's not forget about the silent partner in every single bottle of wine you buy, the government. On top of all those other costs, you have taxes and duties. And we're not talking about pocket change here. In some places, taxes can actually be one of the biggest single parts of that final price you see on the shelf. It's huge. The kind of producer making the wine makes a big difference, too. You've got your estates who grow all their own grapes. They have total control, which is great for quality. But if a hail stom wipes out their crop, they're in big trouble. Then you have merchants who buy grapes or wine from others. This lets them blend for a consistent style year after year. And you can't forget cooperatives, which are super common in Europe. They're basically a collection of growers who pull their resources, which is a lifeline for small farmers. Okay, so we've got a handle on the costs. Now, let's switch gears and talk about the law. When you look at a wine label, what do all those words actually mean? What are they promising you? This is where we dive into the rules of the game. The single most important concept in wine law pretty much everywhere in the world is the geographical indication or GI for short. And really, it's simple. A GI is just a legally protected name for a place. It's a line drawn on a map that guarantees the grapes for that wine were grown inside that line. That's it. It's a promise of origin. Now, here's a number you absolutely have to know, especially for any wine exam. 85%. For a wine to legally put a GI on its label in most parts of the world, at least 85% of the grapes have to come from that specific named region. Remember that number. It's key. So, the European Union, which is, you know, the heartland of wine, takes this GI idea and kicks it up a notch. They've built a whole two-level system that creates a kind of quality pyramid. And you really need to understand how it works. And here are the two tiers. Think of it like this. PDO or protected designation of origin is the top of the pyramid. These are for smaller, more specific, often more famous areas and they come with a super strict rule book. What grapes you can use, how you can make the wine, everything. Then you have PGI, protected geographical indication. This is a much broader category. It covers larger areas and gives wine makers way more freedom and flexibility to experiment. But for that top tier, for a European PDO wine, the rule gets even stricter. It's not 85%, it's 100%. No wiggle room. Every single grape has to come from that defined region. It is an absolute guarantee of origin and authenticity. Now, here's a little tip. You're not always going to see the letters PDIO on the bottle, and that's because the EU lets countries use their own traditional terms. So in France you'll see Appalachian derision control A or AOC. In Italy you'll see the famous DOC or the even higher level DOCG. Just remember these are all different local names for that same top tier PDO category. Okay, last section. Let's bring these two worlds together. We've talked about money. We've talked about law. So how do these legal rules directly impact the price you pay for that bottle? Well, here's the absolute crux of it. When a wine region has a prestigious legal status, like a really strict PDO, those rules do a lot more than just protect its name. They actively restrict how much wine can be made. The laws might demand lower yields in the vineyard or dictate expensive production methods, which raises costs and crucially limits the total supply. And it all comes down to this. It's classic economics 101, really. You have a famous region that everyone wants a piece of. So demand is super high. But at the same time, the strict laws of that region keep the supply very, very limited. And what happens when high demand crashes into low supply? The price has nowhere to go but up. That right there is how law and price come together in the world of wine. So we'll leave you with this to think about. We've seen how the costs pile up and how the law creates scarcity. All of which builds that final price tag. But does that price born from all these rules and market forces actually guarantee that you're going to love what's in the bottle more than something else? Well, that's a question only you can answer. Thanks for joining this explainer.

Now, wine grapes are different than your standard table grapes. They're going to&nbsp;be a little bit smaller, they've got thicker skins, a lot of seeds, they tend to be pretty sweet. What happens when they ripen is the sugars in the grape&nbsp;become more concentrated, and it's those sugars&nbsp;that are going to turn into alcohol through the&nbsp;fermentation process. The yeast is going to eat up&nbsp;all those sugars and poop out alcohol. Because it takes an entire season for these grapes to be&nbsp;ready, there's really only one time a year that&nbsp;you're making wine from those grapes. This is why vintage is important. This one says 2020 - Pinot noir&nbsp;from 2020. That means, in the year 2020, they made&nbsp;this wine. It's going to be different than this&nbsp;same pinot noir made in 2019 or 2017. Every year, based on whatever happened in the climate - in the&nbsp;atmosphere, in the land - that's gonna affect the end&nbsp;result in the bottle. Now, you can blend vintages. In which case you would not put a year on there. A lot of bubbly wines - champagnes - they'll mix vintages. A varietal is the gape. So when you look at your bottle, and it says pinot noir, that's the grape that they're using to make that bottle of wine. However, it&nbsp;doesn't have to be 100% that grape. Now, every country is different. In the United States, I think it has to be 75% of that grape - except in Oregon&nbsp;State it has to be 90%. Now, if they don't meet that&nbsp;minimum, then it becomes a blend. This one here - this is a blend of two varietals. 60% sémillon, and 40% sauvignon blanc. So those are two different grape varietals that they blended together. Does that make sense? You all still with me? They all stem back to one varietal. I didn't even write that in my notes, I don't think, because I thought that's going a little too deep, but I did discover that when i was researching this video. I didn't - I didn't even write it down. The process of&nbsp;making wine. Now each step, everybody can kind of&nbsp;make their own tweaks to it. So that's why you get&nbsp;so many different wines. In general, it's a pretty basic system. You have your grapes - this is what the&nbsp;wine is all about. It comes from these little dots, and they grow, and they become ripe, and then they&nbsp;harvest them. You pick out all the bad ones because, you know, not all the grapes are going to be&nbsp;quite ready or some of them are going to be kind of nasty looking. You get rid of those&nbsp;- you take the good grapes, and then you crush&nbsp;them. This is where red wines and white wines&nbsp; take two separate paths. When making a red wine, they keep the skins with the juice. That's where the color comes from. The color doesn't come from the juice. It comes from the skins. So in the white wines, they remove the skins. Now, there's more to the skins than just color. You also get tannins. Tannins are the things that make wine tannic. And that's gonna cause the drying effect&nbsp; that sometimes you get with a bottle of red wine. Some of them can be really suck-your-face-dry,&nbsp; and that's because of the skins that are left&nbsp;on the grapes when they crush and press&nbsp;and they create what's called the must - and that is what's going to ferment. For the&nbsp;most part, they're going to be between 10 and 15% alcohol. It can only make so much alcohol. You're not gonna get a 90 proof bottle of wine. It just doesn't work that way. There is something called&nbsp;fortified wines, and they add more alcohol, but that's not what we're talking about today. At this point they clarify the wine to remove any bits of dead yeast cells or anything you just&nbsp;don't want in your bottle of wine, and then they can either bottle it straight away or they age it. That's going to alter the flavor. It's going to evolve over time. Sometimes they age it on oak&nbsp;- which means in an oak barrel, and that's going to&nbsp;draw flavors from the oak. I've got in my notes: How old is wine - question mark I'm asking myself the question. It's really old! Thousands of years old! Early civilizations had wine because the&nbsp;fermentation process happens naturally. So&nbsp;they probably just mashed up some grapes, set it off to the side, let nature do its thing, and then drank it. And either got really sick or had a good time. Where is wine made today? It's made all over the place! And there are two, kind of, very general categories as far as locale. You have your Old World wines, which is going to consist of Spain, France, Italy - Wines that have been&nbsp;doing it for, god - ever. Then you have your New World&nbsp;wines, which include North America, South America, Australia, South Africa, New Zealand. You name it. Pretty much anywhere else - that's a New World wine. Now, every place is going to make different wine&nbsp;&nbsp; for a number of reasons. The dirt is different, the&nbsp;weather is different, and this is another reason why wine can be intimidating. You may have a&nbsp;pinot noir from Burgundy, France, and you may&nbsp;have one from Oregon State in the United States. And they're very different. And you may love one, you may hate the other. And then you're like, "I don't know if I like pinot noir. I liked it one&nbsp;time!" And we don't know what this wine - we don't know what this is going to taste like next year. Nobody does! So don't worry - you don't know what's&nbsp;gonna happen in the future. I do think it's pretty&nbsp;impressive, though - those sommeliers. They're - they're&nbsp;actually really impressive. way you would any produce or perishable food product so here's some of the main things you want to keep in mind when it comes to storing your wine first the temperature needs to be stable preferably cooler than warmer if you have a basement that's going to be best if you don't then look for storage space or a closet that fits the bill wherever you do store your wines the temperature shouldn't see at the store sitting in the front window in the sunlight not a good idea why is with corks in them should be stored on their side so this way the cork always stays moist and remains tightly sealed number you want to shoot for is 70% humidity but if you're not too anal about it a basement or crawlspace will suffice clines with screw caps don't need to be stored on their side but all the prior suggestions I made still apply you can actually save your rack space by standing these on their end on a shelf or on the floor and of course over time as you graduate up you may want to opt for a nice
